     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
       Names
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Assyria was also sometimes known as
      <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">
       Subartu
      </a>
      and
      <i>
       Azuhinum
      </i>
      prior to the rise of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_state" title="City state">
       city state
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      after which it was  
      <i>
       <b>
        Aryu
       </b>
      </i>
      , and after its fall, from 605 BC through to the late 7th century AD variously as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athura" title="Athura">
       Athura
      </a>
      and also referenced as
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Atouria&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Atouria (page does not exist)">
       Atouria
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      according to
      <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">
       Strabo
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Syria
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      ),
      <i>
       Assyria
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
       Latin
      </a>
      ) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assuristan" title="Assuristan">
       Assuristan
      </a>
      . After its dissolution in the mid 7th century AD it remained
      <i>
       The Ecclesiastical Province of Ator
      </i>
      . The term
      <i>
       Assyria
      </i>
      can also refer to the geographic region or heartland where Assyria, its empires and the Assyrian people were (and still are) centered. The modern
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christian
      </a>
      (AKA
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldo-Assyrian" title="Chaldo-Assyrian">
       Chaldo-Assyrian
      </a>
      ) ethnic minority in northern
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
       Iraq
      </a>
      , north east
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      , south east Turkey and north west
      <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
       Iran
      </a>
      are the descendants of the ancient Assyrians (see
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_continuity" title="Assyrian continuity">
       Assyrian continuity
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H.W.F._Saggs_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-H.W.F._Saggs-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history_of_Assyria">
       Pre-history of Assyria
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-history of Assyria">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In prehistoric times, the region that was to become known as Assyria (and Subartu) was home to a
      <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">
       Neanderthal
      </a>
      culture such as has been found at the
      <a href="/wiki/Shanidar_Cave" title="Shanidar Cave">
       Shanidar Cave
      </a>
      . The earliest
      <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">
       Neolithic
      </a>
      sites in Assyria were the
      <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">
       Jarmo
      </a>
      culture c. 7100 BC and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hassuna" title="Hassuna">
       Tell Hassuna
      </a>
      , the centre of the
      <i>
       Hassuna culture
      </i>
      , c. 6000 BC.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 3rd millennium BC, a very intimate cultural symbiosis developed between the
      <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">
       Sumerians
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic" title="Semitic">
       Semitic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadians
      </a>
      throughout
      <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
       Mesopotamia
      </a>
      , which included widespread
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bilingualism" title="wikt:bilingualism">
       bilingualism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The influence of
      <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">
       Sumerian
      </a>
      (a
      <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">
       language isolate
      </a>
      , i.e. not related to any other language) on
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      (and vice versa) is evident in all areas, from lexical borrowing on a massive scale, to syntactic, morphological, and phonological convergence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has prompted scholars to refer to Sumerian and Akkadian in the 3rd millennium BC as a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">
        sprachbund
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="755" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg/220px-Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg/330px-Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg/440px-Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Letter_Luenna_Louvre_AO4238.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Letter sent by the high-priest Lu'enna to the king of
        <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">
         Lagash
        </a>
        (maybe
        <a href="/wiki/Urukagina" title="Urukagina">
         Urukagina
        </a>
        ), informing him of his son's death in combat, c. 2400 BC, found in
        <a href="/wiki/Girsu" title="Girsu">
         Girsu
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Akkadian gradually replaced Sumerian as the spoken language of Mesopotamia somewhere after the turn of the 3rd and the 2nd millennium BC (the exact dating being a matter of debate),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-woods-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but Sumerian continued to be used as a sacred, ceremonial, literary and scientific language in Mesopotamia until the 1st century AD.
     </p>
     <p>
      The cities of
      <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
       Assur
      </a>
      (also spelled
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A%C5%A1%C5%A1ur" title="Aur">
       Aur
      </a>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
       Nineveh
      </a>
      , together with a number of other towns and cities, existed since at least before the middle of the 3rd millennium BC (c. 2600 BC), although they appear to have been Sumerian-ruled administrative centres at this time, rather than independent states.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to some
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaeo-Christian" title="Judaeo-Christian">
       Judaeo-Christian
      </a>
      writers
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2014)">
          who?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      , the city of Ashur was founded by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashur_(Bible)" title="Ashur (Bible)">
       Ashur
      </a>
      the son of
      <a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">
       Shem
      </a>
      , who was deified by later generations as the city's patron god
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      . However, it is not among the cities said to have been founded by him in Genesis 10:1112, and the far older Assyrian annals make no mention of the much later Judeo-Christian figures of Shem and Ashur.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyrian tradition lists an early Assyrian king named
      <a href="/wiki/Ushpia" title="Ushpia">
       Ushpia
      </a>
      as having dedicated the first temple to the god Ashur in the city in the 21st century BC. It is highly likely that the city was named in honour of its patron Assyrian god with the same name.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_dating">
       Classical dating
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classical dating">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/George_Syncellus" title="George Syncellus">
       George Syncellus
      </a>
      in his
      <i>
       Chronographia
      </i>
      quotes a fragment from
      <a href="/wiki/Julius_Africanus" title="Julius Africanus">
       Julius Africanus
      </a>
      which dates the founding of Assyria to 2284 BC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Roman historian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Velleius_Paterculus" title="Velleius Paterculus">
       Velleius Paterculus
      </a>
      citing Aemilius Sura states that Assyria was founded 1995 years before
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_V_of_Macedon" title="Philip V of Macedon">
       Philip V
      </a>
      was defeated in 197 BC (at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cynoscephalae" title="Battle of Cynoscephalae">
       Battle of Cynoscephalae
      </a>
      ) by the Romans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The sum therefore 197 + 1995 = 2192 BC for the foundation of Assyria.
      <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">
       Diodorus Siculus
      </a>
      recorded another tradition from
      <a href="/wiki/Ctesias" title="Ctesias">
       Ctesias
      </a>
      , that dates Assyria 1,306 years before 883 BC (the starting date of the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurnasirpal_II" title="Ashurnasirpal II">
       Ashurnasirpal II
      </a>
      ) and so the sum 883 + 1306 = 2189 BC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Eusebius)" title="Chronicon (Eusebius)">
        Chronicle
       </a>
      </i>
      of Eusebius provides yet another date for the founding of Assyria, with the accession of
      <a href="/wiki/Ninus" title="Ninus">
       Ninus
      </a>
      , dating to 2057 BC, but the Armenian translation of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Eusebius)" title="Chronicon (Eusebius)">
        Chronicle
       </a>
      </i>
      puts this figure back slightly to 2116 BC. Another classical dating tradition found in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Excerpta_Latina_Barbari" title="Excerpta Latina Barbari">
        Excerpta Latina Barbari
       </a>
      </i>
      dates the foundation of Assyria, under
      <a href="/wiki/Belus_(Assyrian)" title="Belus (Assyrian)">
       Belus
      </a>
      , to 2206 BC.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Assyria.2C_2600.E2.80.932335_BC">
       Early Assyria, 26002335 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early Assyria, 26002335 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The city of Ashur, together with a number of other Assyrian cities, seem to have been established by 2600 BC, however it is likely that they were initially Sumerian dominated administrative centres. In c. the late 26th century BC,
      <a href="/wiki/Eannatum" title="Eannatum">
       Eannatum
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">
       Lagash
      </a>
      , then the dominant
      <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">
       Sumerian
      </a>
      ruler in Mesopotamia, mentions "smiting
      <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">
       Subartu
      </a>
      " (Subartu being the Sumerian name for Assyria). Similarly, in c. the early 25th century BC,
      <a href="/wiki/Lugal-Anne-Mundu" title="Lugal-Anne-Mundu">
       Lugal-Anne-Mundu
      </a>
      the king of the Sumerian state of
      <a href="/wiki/Adab_(city)" title="Adab (city)">
       Adab
      </a>
      lists Subartu as paying tribute to him.
     </p>
     <p>
      Of the early history of the kingdom of Assyria, little is positively known. In the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_King_List" title="Assyrian King List">
       Assyrian King List
      </a>
      , the earliest king recorded was
      <a href="/wiki/Tudiya" title="Tudiya">
       Tudiya
      </a>
      . In archaeological reports from
      <a href="/wiki/Ebla" title="Ebla">
       Ebla
      </a>
      , it appeared that Tudiya's activities were confirmed with the discovery of a tablet where he concluded a treaty for the operation of a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">
        karum
       </a>
      </i>
      (trading colony) in Eblaite territory, with "king" Ibrium of Ebla (who is now known to have been the
      <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">
       vizier
      </a>
      of Ebla for king
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ishar-Damu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ishar-Damu (page does not exist)">
       Ishar-Damu
      </a>
      ). This entire reading is now questionable, as several scholars have more recently argued that the treaty in question may not have been with king Tudiya of Assyria, but rather with the unnamed king of an uncertain location called "Abarsal".
     </p>
     <p>
      Tudiya was succeeded on the list by
      <a href="/wiki/Adamu" title="Adamu">
       Adamu
      </a>
      and then a further thirteen rulers (Yangi, Shuhlamu, Harharu, Mandaru, Imshu, Harshu, Didanu, Hanu, Zuabu, Nuabu, Abazu, Belu and Azarah). Nothing concrete is yet known about these names, although it has been noted that a much later Babylonian tablet listing the ancestral lineage of
      <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">
       Hammurabi
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorite" title="Amorite">
       Amorite
      </a>
      king of Babylon, seems to have copied the same names from Tudiya through Nuabu, though in a heavily corrupted form.
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest kings, such as Tudiya, who are recorded as
      <i>
       kings who lived in tents
      </i>
      , were independent semi-nomadic pastoralist rulers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_2-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These kings at some point became fully urbanised and founded the
      <i>
       city state
      </i>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_in_the_Akkadian_Empire_and_Neo-Sumerian_Empires">
       Assyria in the Akkadian Empire and Neo-Sumerian Empires
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Assyria in the Akkadian Empire and Neo-Sumerian Empires">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      During the
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">
       Akkadian Empire
      </a>
      (23342154 BC) the Assyrians, like all the Mesopotamian Semites (and also the Sumerians), became subject to the dynasty of the city state of
      <a href="/wiki/Akkad_(city)" title="Akkad (city)">
       Akkad
      </a>
      , centered in central Mesopotamia. The Akkadian Empire founded by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sargon_the_Great" title="Sargon the Great">
       Sargon the Great
      </a>
      , claimed to encompass the surrounding "four quarters". The region of Assyria, north of the seat of the empire in central Mesopotamia had also been known as
      <a href="/wiki/Subartu" title="Subartu">
       Subartu
      </a>
      by the Sumerians, and the name
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Azuhinum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Azuhinum (page does not exist)">
       Azuhinum
      </a>
      in Akkadian records also seems to refer to Assyria proper.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyrian rulers were subject to Sargon and his successors, and the city of Ashur became a regional administrative center of the Empire, implicated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Nuzi#Archaeology" title="Nuzi">
       Nuzi tablets
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shendge1997_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shendge1997-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During this period, the Akkadian-speaking Semites of Mesopotamia came to rule an empire encompassing not only Mesopotamia itself but large swathes of Asia Minor, ancient Iran, Elam, the
      <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">
       Arabian Peninsula
      </a>
      , Canaan and Syria.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria seems to have already been firmly involved in trade in Asia Minor by this time; the earliest known reference to Anatolian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">
        karum
       </a>
      </i>
      s in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_empire" title="Hittite empire">
       Hatti
      </a>
      , was found on later cuneiform tablets describing the early period of the Akkadian Empire (c. 2350 BC). On those tablets, Assyrian traders in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burushanda" title="Burushanda">
       Burushanda
      </a>
      implored the help of their ruler, Sargon the Great, and this appellation continued to exist throughout the Assyrian Empire for about 1,700 years. The name "Hatti" itself even appears in later accounts of his grandson,
      <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">
       Naram-Sin
      </a>
      , campaigning in Anatolia.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyrian and Akkadian traders spread the use of writing in the form of the Mesopotamian
      <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">
       cuneiform
      </a>
      script to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
       Asia Minor
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Levant" title="The Levant">
       The Levant
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">
       Lebanon
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      However, towards the end of the reign of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sargon_the_Great" title="Sargon the Great">
       Sargon the Great
      </a>
      , the Assyrian faction rebelled against him; "the tribes of Assyria of the upper countryin their turn attacked, but they submitted to his arms, and Sargon settled their habitations, and he smote them grievously".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Akkadian Empire was destroyed by economic decline and internal civil war, followed by attacks from barbarian
      <a href="/wiki/Gutian_people" title="Gutian people">
       Gutian people
      </a>
      in 2154 BC.
     </p>
     <p>
      The rulers of Assyria during the period between c. 2154 BC and 2112 BC once again became fully independent, as the Gutians are only known to have administered southern Mesopotamia. However, the king list is the only information from Assyria for this period.
     </p>
     <p>
      Most of Assyria briefly became part of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Sumerian_Empire" title="Neo-Sumerian Empire">
       Neo-Sumerian Empire
      </a>
      (or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3rd_dynasty_of_Ur" title="3rd dynasty of Ur">
       3rd dynasty of Ur
      </a>
      ) founded in c. 2112 BC. Sumerian domination extended as far as the city of Ashur, but appears not to have reached Nineveh and the far north of Assyria. One local ruler (
      <i>
       shakkanakku
      </i>
      ) named
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Z%C4%81riqum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zriqum (page does not exist)">
       Zriqum
      </a>
      (who does not appear on any Assyrian king list) is listed as paying tribute to
      <a href="/wiki/Amar-Sin" title="Amar-Sin">
       Amar-Sin
      </a>
      of Ur. Ashur's rulers appear to have remained largely under Sumerian domination until the mid-21st century BC (c. 2050 BC); the king list names Assyrian rulers for this period and several are known from other references to have also borne the title of
      <i>
       shakkanakka
      </i>
      or vassal governors for the neo-Sumerians.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Assyrian_Kingdom">
       Old Assyrian Kingdom
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Old Assyrian Kingdom">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The first written inscriptions by 'urbanised' Assyrian kings appear in the mid-21st century BC, after they had shrugged off Sumerian domination. The land of Assyria as a whole then consisted of a number of city states and small Semitic kingdoms, some of which were initially independent of Assyria. The foundation of the first major temple in the city of Ashur was traditionally ascribed to king
      <a href="/wiki/Ushpia" title="Ushpia">
       Ushpia
      </a>
      who reigned c. 2050 BC, possibly a contemporary of
      <a href="/wiki/Ishbi-Erra" title="Ishbi-Erra">
       Ishbi-Erra
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">
       Isin
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Naplanum" title="Naplanum">
       Naplanum
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">
       Larsa
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He was reputedly succeeded by kings named
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apiashal" title="Apiashal">
       Apiashal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sulili" title="Sulili">
       Sulili
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kikkiya" title="Kikkiya">
       Kikkiya
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Akiya" title="Akiya">
       Akiya
      </a>
      (died c. 2026 BC), of whom little is known, apart from much later mentions of Kikkiya conducting fortifications on the city walls, and building work on temples in Ashur.
     </p>
     <p>
      The main rivals, neighbours or trading partners to early Assyrian kings during the 22nd, 21st and 20th centuries BC would have been the
      <a href="/wiki/Hattians" title="Hattians">
       Hattians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">
       Hurrians
      </a>
      to the north in Asia Minor, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutians" title="Gutians">
       Gutians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lullubi" title="Lullubi">
       Lullubi
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turukku" title="Turukku">
       Turukku
      </a>
      to the east in the
      <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">
       Zagros Mountains
      </a>
      of northwest
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" title="Ancient Iran">
       Ancient Iran
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      to the southeast in what is now south central Iran, the
      <a href="/wiki/Amorites" title="Amorites">
       Amorites
      </a>
      to the west in what is today Syria, and their fellow
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumero-Akkadian" title="Sumero-Akkadian">
       Sumero-Akkadian
      </a>
      city-states of southern Mesopotamia such as
      <a href="/wiki/Isin" title="Isin">
       Isin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">
       Kish
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">
       Ur
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">
       Eshnunna
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">
       Larsa
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_2-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Like many city-states in early Mesopotamian history, Ashur was, to a great extent, originally an
      <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">
       oligarchy
      </a>
      rather than a monarchy. Authority was considered to lie with "the City", and the polity had three main centres of poweran assembly of elders, a hereditary ruler, and an
      <a href="/wiki/Eponym" title="Eponym">
       eponym
      </a>
      . The ruler presided over the assembly and carried out its decisions. He was not referred to with the usual Akkadian term for "king",
      <i>
       arrum
      </i>
      /
      <i>
       Sharru
      </i>
      ; that was instead reserved for the city's patron deity
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assur_(god)" title="Assur (god)">
       Assur
      </a>
      , of whom the ruler was the high priest. The ruler himself was only designated as "the steward of Assur" (
      <i>
       iiak Assur
      </i>
      ), where the term for steward is a borrowing from Sumerian
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ENSI" title="ENSI">
        ensi(k)
       </a>
      </i>
      . The third centre of power was the eponym (
      <i>
       limmum
      </i>
      ), who gave the year his name, similarly to the later
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archons_of_Athens" title="Archons of Athens">
       archons
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">
       consuls
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" title="Classical Antiquity">
       Classical Antiquity
      </a>
      . He was annually elected by
      <a href="/wiki/Sortition" title="Sortition">
       lot
      </a>
      and was responsible for the economic administration of the city, which included the power to detain people and confiscate property. The institution of the eponym as well as the formula
      <i>
       iiak Assur
      </i>
      lingered on as ceremonial vestiges of this early system throughout the history of the Assyrian monarchy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dynasty_of_Puzur-Ashur_I.2C_2025.E2.80.931809_BC.2C_Old_Assyrian_Empire">
       Dynasty of Puzur-Ashur I, 20251809 BC, Old Assyrian Empire
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Dynasty of Puzur-Ashur I, 20251809 BC, Old Assyrian Empire">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In approximately 2025 BC (long chronology),
      <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_I" title="Puzur-Ashur I">
       Puzur-Ashur I
      </a>
      (perhaps a contemporary of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shu-ilishu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shu-ilishu (page does not exist)">
       Shu-ilishu
      </a>
      of Larsa and
      <a href="/wiki/Samium" title="Samium">
       Samium
      </a>
      of Isin) is speculated to have overthrown Kikkiya and founded a new dynasty which was to survive for 216 years. His descendants left inscriptions mentioning him regarding the building of temples to gods such as Ashur,
      <a href="/wiki/Adad" title="Adad">
       Adad
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" title="Ishtar">
       Ishtar
      </a>
      in Assyria. The length of his reign is unknown.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Shalim-ahum" title="Shalim-ahum">
       Shalim-ahum
      </a>
      (died c. 2009 BC) succeeded the throne at a currently unknown date. He left inscriptions in archaic Old Assyrian regarding the construction of a temple dedicated to the god Ashur, and the placement of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Beer_vats&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Beer vats (page does not exist)">
       beer vats
      </a>
      within it.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilushuma" title="Ilushuma">
       Ilushuma
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (c. 20081975 BC) took the throne in c. 2008 BC, and is known from his inscription (extant in several copies) where he claims to have "washed the copper" and "established liberty" for the Akkadians in Sumerian city-states as far as the Persian Gulf. This has been taken by some scholars to imply that he made military campaigns into Southern Mesopotamia to relieve his fellow Mesopotamians from Amorite and Elamite invasions, however some recent scholars have taken the view that the inscription means he supplied these areas with copper from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_empire" title="Hittite empire">
       Hatti
      </a>
      , and that the word used for "liberty" (
      <i>
       adduraru
      </i>
      ) is usually in the context of his exempting the southern Mesopotamian kings from tariffs.
     </p>
     <p>
      "The freedom
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        nb 1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      of the Akkadians and their children I established. I purified their copper. I established their freedom from the border of the marshes and Ur and Nippur,
      <a href="/wiki/Awal" title="Awal">
       Awal
      </a>
      , and Kish,
      <a href="/wiki/Der_(Sumer)" title="Der (Sumer)">
       Der
      </a>
      of the goddess Ishtar, as far as the City of (Ashur)."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grayson_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-grayson-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria had long held extensive contact with Hattian, Hittite and Hurrian cities on the Anatolian plateau in Asia Minor. The Assyrians who had for centuries traded in the region, and possibly ruled small areas bordering Assyria, now established significant colonies in
      <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">
       Cappadocia
      </a>
      (e.g., at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanesh" title="Kanesh">
       Kanesh
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCltepe" title="Kltepe">
       Kltepe
      </a>
      ) from 2008 BC to 1740 BC). These colonies, called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">
        karum
       </a>
      </i>
      , from the Akkadian word for 'port', were attached to Hattian cities in Anatolia, but physically separate, and had special tax status. They must have arisen from a long tradition of trade between Assyria and the Anatolian cities, but no archaeological or written records show this. The trade consisted of metals (copper or tin and perhaps iron; the terminology is not entirely clear) being traded for textiles from Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Erishum_I" title="Erishum I">
       Erishum I
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (c. 19741935 BC) vigorously expanded Assyrian colonies in Asia Minor during his long reign, the major ones appearing to be at Kanesh,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1a" title="attua">
       attua
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bo%C4%9Fazk%C3%B6y" title="Boazky">
       Boazky
      </a>
      ) (the future capital of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" title="Hittite Empire">
       Hittite Empire
      </a>
      ) and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Amkuwa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Amkuwa (page does not exist)">
       Amkuwa
      </a>
      (
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alisar_H%C3%B6y%C3%BCk&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alisar Hyk (page does not exist)">
       Alisar Hyk
      </a>
      ), together with a further eighteen smaller colonies. He created some of the earliest examples of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Written_Law" title="Written Law">
       Written Law
      </a>
      , conducted extensive building work in the form of fortifying the walls of major Assyrian cities and the erection of temples dedicated to Ashur and Ishtar. It is from his reign that the continuous
      <i>
       limmum
      </i>
      lists are known, however there are references to the eponym-books for his predecessors having been destroyed at some point.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ikunum" title="Ikunum">
       Ikunum
      </a>
      (c. 19341921 BC)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      built a major temple for the god
      <a href="/wiki/Ningal" title="Ningal">
       Ningal
      </a>
      . He further strengthened the fortifications of the city of Assur and maintained Assyria's colonies in Asia Minor.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Sargon_I" title="Sargon I">
       Sargon I
      </a>
      (c. 19201881 BC)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      succeeded him in c. 1920 BC, and had an unusually long reign of 39 years. It is likely he was named after his illustrious predecessor
      <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">
       Sargon of Akkad
      </a>
      . He is known to have refortified the defences of major Assyrian cities, and maintained Assyrian colonies in Asia Minor during his reign. Apart from this, little has yet been unearthed about him. At some point he appears to have withdrawn Assyrian aid to southern Mesopotamia. It was during his reign in Assyria that the initially minor city-state of
      <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
       Babylon
      </a>
      was founded in 1894 BC by an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorite" title="Amorite">
       Amorite
      </a>
      <i>
       Malka
      </i>
      (prince) named
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumuabum" title="Sumuabum">
       Sumuabum
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_II" title="Puzur-Ashur II">
       Puzur-Ashur II
      </a>
      (c. 18811873 BC) came to the throne as an already older man due to his fathers long reign. Little is known about his rule, but it appears to have been uneventful.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Assyria" title="Naram-Sin of Assyria">
       Naram-Suen
      </a>
      (c. 18721818 BC) ascended to the throne in 1872 BC, and is likely named after his predecessor
      <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Akkad" title="Naram-Sin of Akkad">
       Naram-Sin
      </a>
      of the Akkadian Empire. Assyria continued to be wealthy during his 54-year-long reign (one of the longest in the ancient Near East), and he defeated the future usurper king
      <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">
       Shamshi-Adad I
      </a>
      who attempted to take his throne.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Erishum_II" title="Erishum II">
       Erishum II
      </a>
      (c. 18181809 BC) was to be the last king of the dynasty of Puzur-Ashur I, founded c. 2025 BC. After only eight or nine years in power he was overthrown by Shamshi-Adad I, the Amorite usurper who had previously been defeated in an attempt to unseat
      <a href="/wiki/Naram-Sin_of_Assyria" title="Naram-Sin of Assyria">
       Naram-Suen
      </a>
      , and who claimed legitimacy by asserting descent from the mid 21st century BC Assyrian king,
      <a href="/wiki/Ushpia" title="Ushpia">
       Ushpia
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Amorite_Period_in_Assyria.2C_1809.E2.80.931750_BC">
       Amorite Period in Assyria, 18091750 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Amorite Period in Assyria, 18091750 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Amorites were successfully repelled by the Assyrian kings of the 20th and 19th centuries BC. However, in 1809 BC the native Mesopotamian king of Assyria
      <a href="/wiki/Erishum_II" title="Erishum II">
       Erishum II
      </a>
      was deposed, and the throne of Assyria was usurped by
      <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">
       Shamshi-Adad I
      </a>
      (c. 1809  1776 BC) in the expansion of Semitic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorite" title="Amorite">
       Amorite
      </a>
      tribes from the
      <a href="/wiki/Khabur_River" title="Khabur River">
       Khabur River
      </a>
      delta in the north eastern Levant.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although regarded as an Amorite by later Assyrian tradition, Shamshi-Adad's descent is suggested to be from the same line as the native Mesopotamian ruler Ushpia in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_King_List" title="Assyrian King List">
       Assyrian King List
      </a>
      . He put his son
      <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan" title="Ishme-Dagan">
       Ishme-Dagan
      </a>
      on the throne of a nearby Assyrian city,
      <a href="/wiki/Ekallatum" title="Ekallatum">
       Ekallatum
      </a>
      , and maintained Assyria's Anatolian colonies. Shamshi-Adad I then went on to conquer the kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Mari,_Syria" title="Mari, Syria">
       Mari
      </a>
      (in modern Syria) on the
      <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">
       Euphrates
      </a>
      putting another of his sons,
      <a href="/wiki/Yasmah-Adad" title="Yasmah-Adad">
       Yasmah-Adad
      </a>
      on the throne there. Shamshi-Adad's Assyria now encompassed the whole of northern Mesopotamia and included territory in central Mesopotamia, Asia Minor and northern Syria. Shamshi-Adad I mentions conducting raids on the
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaanite
      </a>
      coasts of the far off
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">
       Mediterranean
      </a>
      , where he erected stelae to commemorate his victories. He himself resided in a new capital city founded in the Khabur valley in northern Mesopotamia, called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shubat-Enlil" title="Shubat-Enlil">
       Shubat-Enlil
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan_I" title="Ishme-Dagan I">
       Ishme-Dagan I
      </a>
      (17741763 BC) inherited Assyria, but Yasmah-Adad was overthrown by a new king called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zimrilim" title="Zimrilim">
       Zimrilim
      </a>
      in Mari. The new king of Mari allied himself with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorite" title="Amorite">
       Amorite
      </a>
      king
      <a href="/wiki/Hammurabi" title="Hammurabi">
       Hammurabi
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
       Babylon
      </a>
      , who had made the recently created, and originally minor state of Babylon into a major power. It was from the reign of Hammurabi onwards that southern Mesopotamia came to be known as
      <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
       Babylonia
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria now faced the rising power of Babylon in the south. Ishme-Dagan responded by making an alliance with the enemies of Babylon, and the power struggle continued without resolution for decades. Ishme-Dagan, like his father was a great warrior, and in addition to repelling Babylonian attacks, campaigned successfully against the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turukku" title="Turukku">
       Turukku
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Lullubi" title="Lullubi">
       Lullubi
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">
       Zagros Mountains
      </a>
      (in modern Iran) who had attacked the Assyrian city of
      <a href="/wiki/Ekallatum" title="Ekallatum">
       Ekallatum
      </a>
      , and against
      <a href="/wiki/Dadusha" title="Dadusha">
       Dadusha
      </a>
      , king of
      <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">
       Eshnunna
      </a>
      , and the state of
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Iamhad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Iamhad (page does not exist)">
       Iamhad
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">
       Aleppo
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_under_Babylonian_domination.2C_1750.E2.80.931732_BC">
       Assyria under Babylonian domination, 17501732 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Assyria under Babylonian domination, 17501732 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Hammurabi, after first conquering Mari, Larsa, and
      <a href="/wiki/Eshnunna" title="Eshnunna">
       Eshnunna
      </a>
      , eventually prevailed over Ishme-Dagan's successor
      <a href="/wiki/Mut-Ashkur" title="Mut-Ashkur">
       Mut-Ashkur
      </a>
      (17501740 BC), and subjected him to Babylon c. 1750 BC. With Hammurabi, the various
      <i>
       karum
      </i>
      colonies in Anatolia ceased trade activityprobably because the goods of Assyria were now being traded with the Babylonians. The Assyrian monarchy survived, however the three Amorite kings succeeding Ishme-Dagan,
      <a href="/wiki/Mut-Ashkur" title="Mut-Ashkur">
       Mut-Ashkur
      </a>
      (who was the son of Ishme-Dagan and married to a Hurrian queen),
      <a href="/wiki/Rimush" title="Rimush">
       Rimush
      </a>
      (17391733 BC) and
      <a href="/wiki/Asinum" title="Asinum">
       Asinum
      </a>
      (1732 BC), were vassals, dependent on the Babylonians during the reign of Hammurabi, and for a short time, of his successor
      <a href="/wiki/Samsu-iluna" title="Samsu-iluna">
       Samsu-iluna
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_Adaside_dynasty.2C_1732.E2.80.931451_BC">
       Assyrian Adaside dynasty, 17321451 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Assyrian Adaside dynasty, 17321451 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The short lived
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_Empire" title="Babylonian Empire">
       Babylonian Empire
      </a>
      quickly began to unravel upon the death of Hammurabi, and Babylonia lost control over Assyria during the reign of Hammurabi's successor
      <a href="/wiki/Samsu-iluna" title="Samsu-iluna">
       Samsu-iluna
      </a>
      (17501712 BC). A period of civil war ensued after Asinum (a grandson of Shamshi-Adad I and the last Amorite ruler of Assyria) was deposed in approximately 1732 BC by a powerful native Assyrian vice regent named
      <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Sin" title="Puzur-Sin">
       Puzur-Sin
      </a>
      , who regarded Asinum as both a foreigner and a former lackey of Babylon.
     </p>
     <p>
      A native king named
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-dugul" title="Ashur-dugul">
       Ashur-dugul
      </a>
      seized the throne in 1732 BC, probably with the help of Puzur-Sin. However, he was unable to retain control for long, and was soon deposed by a rival claimant,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashur-apla-idi" title="Ashur-apla-idi">
       Ashur-apla-idi
      </a>
      . Internal instability ensued with four further kings (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasir-Sin" title="Nasir-Sin">
       Nasir-Sin
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sin-namir" title="Sin-namir">
       Sin-namir
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ipqi-Ishtar" title="Ipqi-Ishtar">
       Ipqi-Ishtar
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adad-salulu" title="Adad-salulu">
       Adad-salulu
      </a>
      ) all reigning in quick succession over a period of approximately six years between 1732 and 1727 BC. Babylonia seems to have been too powerless to intervene or take advantage of this situation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Finally, a king named
      <a href="/wiki/Adasi" title="Adasi">
       Adasi
      </a>
      (17261701 BC) came to the fore c. 1726 BC and managed to quell the civil unrest and stabilise the situation in Assyria. Adasi completely drove the Babylonians and Amorites from the Assyrian sphere of influence during his reign, and Babylonian power began to quickly wane in Mesopotamia as a whole, also losing the far south of Mesopotamia (an area roughly corresponding to ancient
      <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">
       Sumer
      </a>
      ) to the native Akkadian-speaking
      <a href="/wiki/Sealand_Dynasty" title="Sealand Dynasty">
       Sealand Dynasty
      </a>
      , although the Amorites would retain control over a much reduced and weak Babylonia itself until 1595 BC, when they were overthrown by the
      <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
       Kassites
      </a>
      , a people from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zagros" title="Zagros">
       Zagros
      </a>
      Mountains who spoke a
      <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">
       language isolate
      </a>
      and were neither Semites nor Indo-Europeans.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dr%C3%A4kt,_Assyrier,_Nordisk_familjebok.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="745" data-file-width="352" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Dr%C3%A4kt%2C_Assyrier%2C_Nordisk_familjebok.png/130px-Dr%C3%A4kt%2C_Assyrier%2C_Nordisk_familjebok.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Dr%C3%A4kt%2C_Assyrier%2C_Nordisk_familjebok.png/195px-Dr%C3%A4kt%2C_Assyrier%2C_Nordisk_familjebok.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Dr%C3%A4kt%2C_Assyrier%2C_Nordisk_familjebok.png/260px-Dr%C3%A4kt%2C_Assyrier%2C_Nordisk_familjebok.png 2x" width="130"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dr%C3%A4kt,_Assyrier,_Nordisk_familjebok.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Assyrian, 1400 BC
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Adasi was succeeded by
      <a href="/wiki/Bel-bani" title="Bel-bani">
       Bel-bani
      </a>
      (17001691 BC) who is credited in Assyrian annals with inflicting further defeats on the Babylonians and Amorites, and further strengthening and stabilising the kingdom.
     </p>
     <p>
      Little is currently known of many of the kings that followed such as;
      <a href="/wiki/Libaya" title="Libaya">
       Libaya
      </a>
      (16901674 BC),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharma-Adad_I" title="Sharma-Adad I">
       Sharma-Adad I
      </a>
      (16731662 BC),
      <a href="/wiki/Iptar-Sin" title="Iptar-Sin">
       Iptar-Sin
      </a>
      (16611650 BC),
      <a href="/wiki/Bazaya" title="Bazaya">
       Bazaya
      </a>
      (16491622 BC) (a contemporary of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peshgaldaramesh" title="Peshgaldaramesh">
       Peshgaldaramesh
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sealand_Dynasty" title="Sealand Dynasty">
       Sealand Dynasty
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Lullaya" title="Lullaya">
       Lullaya
      </a>
      (16211618 BC) (who usurperped the throne from Bazaya),
      <a href="/wiki/Shu-Ninua" title="Shu-Ninua">
       Shu-Ninua
      </a>
      (16151602 BC) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharma-Adad_II" title="Sharma-Adad II">
       Sharma-Adad II
      </a>
      (16011599 BC). However, Assyria seems to have been a relatively strong and stable nation, existing undisturbed by its neighbours such as the Hatti, Hittites, Hurrians, Amorites, Babylonians, Elamites or Mitanni during this period.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria remained strong and secure; when Babylon was sacked and its Amorite rulers deposed by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_Empire" title="Hittite Empire">
       Hittite Empire
      </a>
      , and subsequently fell to the
      <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
       Kassites
      </a>
      in 1595 BC, both powers were unable to make any inroads into Assyria, and there seems to have been no trouble between the first Kassite ruler of Babylon,
      <a href="/wiki/Agum_II" title="Agum II">
       Agum II
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erishum_III" title="Erishum III">
       Erishum III
      </a>
      (15981586 BC) of Assyria, and a mutually beneficial treaty was signed between the two rulers.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_II" title="Shamshi-Adad II">
       Shamshi-Adad II
      </a>
      (15851580 BC),
      <a href="/wiki/Ishme-Dagan_II" title="Ishme-Dagan II">
       Ishme-Dagan II
      </a>
      (15791562 BC) and
      <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_III" title="Shamshi-Adad III">
       Shamshi-Adad III
      </a>
      (15621548 BC) seem also to have had peaceful tenures, although few records have thus far been discovered about their reigns. Similarly,
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nirari_I" title="Ashur-nirari I">
       Ashur-nirari I
      </a>
      (15471522 BC) seems not to have been troubled by the newly founded Mitanni Empire in Asia Minor, the Hittite empire, or Babylon during his 25-year reign. He is known to have been an active king, improving the infrastructure, dedicating temples and conducting various building projects throughout the kingdom.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Puzur-Ashur_III" title="Puzur-Ashur III">
       Puzur-Ashur III
      </a>
      (15211498 BC) proved to be a strong and energetic ruler. He undertook much rebuilding work in Assur, the city was refortified and the southern quarters incorporated into the main city defences. Temples to the moon god
      <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">
       Sin
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Sin_(mythology)" title="Sin (mythology)">
       Nanna
      </a>
      ) and the sun god
      <a href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">
       Shamash
      </a>
      were erected during his reign. He signed a treaty with
      <a href="/wiki/Burnaburiash_I" title="Burnaburiash I">
       Burna-Buriash I
      </a>
      the Kassite king of Babylon, defining the borders of the two nations in the late 16th century BC. He was succeeded by
      <a href="/wiki/Enlil-nasir_I" title="Enlil-nasir I">
       Enlil-nasir I
      </a>
      (14971483 BC) who appears to have had a peaceful an uneventful reign, as does his successor
      <a href="/wiki/Nur-ili" title="Nur-ili">
       Nur-ili
      </a>
      (14821471 BC).
     </p>
     <p>
      The son of Nur-ili,
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-shaduni" title="Ashur-shaduni">
       Ashur-shaduni
      </a>
      (1470 BC) was deposed by his uncle
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-rabi_I" title="Ashur-rabi I">
       Ashur-rabi I
      </a>
      (14701451 BC) in his first year of rule. Little is known about his nineteen-year reign, but it appears to have been largely uneventful.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_in_decline.2C_1450.E2.80.931393_BC">
       Assyria in decline, 14501393 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Assyria in decline, 14501393 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The emergence of the Mitanni Empire in the 16th century BC did eventually lead to a short period of sporadic Mitanni-Hurrian domination in the latter half of the 15th century. The Indo-European-speaking Mitanni are thought to have conquered and formed the ruling class over the indigenous Hurrians of eastern Anatolia. The Hurrians spoke a
      <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">
       language isolate
      </a>
      , i.e. neither Semitic nor Indo-European.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nadin-ahhe_I" title="Ashur-nadin-ahhe I">
       Ashur-nadin-ahhe I
      </a>
      (14501431 BC) was courted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egyptians
      </a>
      , who were rivals of the Mitanni, and attempting to gain a foothold in the
      <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">
       Near East
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_II" title="Amenhotep II">
       Amenhotep II
      </a>
      sent the Assyrian king a tribute of gold to seal an alliance against the Hurri-Mitanni empire. It is likely that this alliance prompted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saushtatar" title="Saushtatar">
       Saushtatar
      </a>
      , the Mitanni emperor, to invade Assyria, and sack the city of Ashur, after which Assyria became a sometime vassal state, with Ashur-nadin-ahhe I being forced to pay tribute to Saushtatar. He was deposed by his own brother
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enlil-nasir_II" title="Enlil-nasir II">
       Enlil-nasir II
      </a>
      (14301425 BC) in 1430 BC, possibly with the aid of the Mitanni, who received tribute from the new king.
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nirari_II" title="Ashur-nirari II">
       Ashur-nirari II
      </a>
      (14241418 BC) had an uneventful reign, and appears to have also paid tribute to the Mitanni Empire.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_2-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrian monarchy survived, and the Mitanni influence appears to have been sporadic. They appear not to have been always willing or indeed able to interfere in Assyrian internal and international affairs.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-bel-nisheshu" title="Ashur-bel-nisheshu">
       Ashur-bel-nisheshu
      </a>
      (14171409 BC) seems to have been independent of Mitanni influence, as evidenced by his signing a mutually beneficial treaty with
      <a href="/wiki/Karaindash" title="Karaindash">
       Karaindash
      </a>
      , the Kassite king of Babylonia in the late 15th century. He also undertook extensive rebuilding work in Ashur itself, and Assyria appears to have redeveloped its former highly sophisticated financial and economic systems during his reign.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-rim-nisheshu" title="Ashur-rim-nisheshu">
       Ashur-rim-nisheshu
      </a>
      (14081401 BC) also undertook building work, strengthening the city walls of the capital.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-nadin-ahhe_II" title="Ashur-nadin-ahhe II">
       Ashur-nadin-ahhe II
      </a>
      (14001393 BC) also received a tribute of gold and diplomatic overtures from Egypt, probably in an attempt to gain Assyrian military support against Egypt's Mitanni and Hittite rivals in the region. However, the Assyrian king appears not to have been in a strong enough position to challenge the Mitanni or Hittites.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Eriba-Adad_I" title="Eriba-Adad I">
       Eriba-Adad I
      </a>
      (13921366 BC), a son of Ashur-bel-nisheshu, ascended the throne in 1392 BC and finally broke the ties to the Mitanni Empire.
     </p>
     <p>
      There are dozens of Mesopotamian cuneiform texts from this period, with precise observations of solar and lunar eclipses, that have been used as 'anchors' in the various attempts to define the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronology_of_Babylonia_and_Assyria" title="Chronology of Babylonia and Assyria">
       chronology of Babylonia and Assyria
      </a>
      for the early 2nd millennium BC (i.e., the "high", "middle", and "low" chronologies.)
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Assyrian_Empire.2C_1392.E2.80.931056_BC">
       Middle Assyrian Empire, 13921056 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Middle Assyrian Empire, 13921056 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="infobox geography vcard vevent" style="width:290px;">

         </a>

      <tr class="mergedrow">

       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr class="mergedtoprow">

        <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">
         Mitanni
        </a>
       </td>
       <td style="vertical-align: bottom;">
        1392 BC
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr class="mergedbottomrow">
       <td style="width:1.0em; padding:0 0 0 0.6em;">

       </td>

        </a>

        934 BC
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Scholars variously date the beginning of the "Middle Assyrian period" to either the fall of the Old Assyrian kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Shamshi-Adad_I" title="Shamshi-Adad I">
       Shamshi-Adad I
      </a>
      , or to the ascension of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_I" title="Ashur-uballit I">
       Ashur-uballit I
      </a>
      to the throne of Assyria.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_expansion_and_empire.2C_1392.E2.80.931056_BC">
       Assyrian expansion and empire, 13921056 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Assyrian expansion and empire, 13921056 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_the_Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Military history of the Neo-Assyrian Empire">
       Military history of the Neo-Assyrian Empire
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Assyria had difficulties with keeping the trade routes open. Unlike the situation in the Old Assyrian period, the Anatolian metal trade was effectively dominated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">
       Hittites
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">
       Hurrians
      </a>
      . These people now controlled the Mediterranean ports, while the
      <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
       Kassites
      </a>
      controlled the river route south to the
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">
       Persian Gulf
      </a>
      . The main Assyrian cities of the middle period were
      <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalhu" title="Kalhu">
       Kalhu
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">
       Nimrud
      </a>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
       Nineveh
      </a>
      , all situated in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">
       Tigris
      </a>
      River valley. At the end of the Bronze Age, Nineveh was much smaller than Babylon, but still one of the world's major cities (population c. 33,000). By the end of the Neo-Assyrian period, it had grown to a population of some 120,000, and was possibly the largest city in the world at that time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Middle Assyrian kingdom was well organized, and in the firm control of the king, who also functioned as the High Priest of
      <a href="/wiki/Anshar" title="Anshar">
       Ashur
      </a>
      , the state god. He had certain obligations to fulfill in the cult, and had to provide resources for the temples. The priesthood became a major power in Assyrian society. Conflicts with the priesthood are thought to have been behind the murder of king
      <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">
       Tukulti-Ninurta I
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      By the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Eriba-Adad_I" title="Eriba-Adad I">
       Eriba-Adad I
      </a>
      (13921366 BC) Mitanni influence over Assyria was on the wane. Eriba-Adad I became involved in a dynastic battle between
      <a href="/wiki/Tushratta" title="Tushratta">
       Tushratta
      </a>
      and his brother
      <a href="/wiki/Artatama_II" title="Artatama II">
       Artatama II
      </a>
      and after this his son
      <a href="/wiki/Shuttarna_II" title="Shuttarna II">
       Shuttarna II
      </a>
      , who called himself king of the Hurri while seeking support from the Assyrians. A pro-Assyria faction appeared at the royal Mitanni court. Eriba-Adad I had thus finally broken Mitanni influence over Assyria, and in turn had now made Assyria an influence over Mitanni affairs.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_I" title="Ashur-uballit I">
       Ashur-uballit I
      </a>
      (13651330 BC) succeeded the throne of Assyria in 1365 BC, and proved to be a fierce, ambitious and powerful ruler. Assyrian pressure from the southeast and Hittite pressure from the north-west, enabled Ashur-uballit I to break Mitanni power. He met and decisively defeated Shuttarna II, the Mitanni king in battle, making Assyria once more an imperial power at the expense of not only the Mitanni themselves, but also Kassite Babylonia, the Hurrians and the Hittites; and a time came when the Kassite king in Babylon was glad to marry
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Muballi%E1%B9%ADat-%C5%A0%C4%93r%C5%ABa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Muballiat-ra (page does not exist)">
       Muballiat-ra
      </a>
      , the daughter of Ashur-uballit, whose letters to
      <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">
       Akhenaten
      </a>
      of Egypt form part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Amarna_letters" title="Amarna letters">
       Amarna letters
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      This marriage led to disastrous results for Babylonia, as the Kassite faction at court murdered the half Assyrian Babylonian king and placed a pretender on the throne. Assur-uballit I promptly invaded Babylonia to avenge his son-in-law, entering Babylon, deposing the king and installing
      <a href="/wiki/Kurigalzu_II" title="Kurigalzu II">
       Kurigalzu II
      </a>
      of the royal line king there.
     </p>
     <p>
      Ashur-uballit I then attacked and defeated
      <a href="/wiki/Shattiwaza" title="Shattiwaza">
       Mattiwaza
      </a>
      , the Mitanni king, despite attempts by the Hittite king
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suppiluliumas" title="Suppiluliumas">
       Suppiluliumas
      </a>
      , now fearful of growing Assyrian power, to help the Mitanni. The lands of the Mitanni and Hurrians were duly appropriated by Assyria, making it a large and powerful empire.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Enlil-nirari" title="Enlil-nirari">
       Enlil-nirari
      </a>
      (13291308 BC) succeeded Ashur-uballit I. He described himself as a "Great-King" (
      <i>
       Sharru rab
      </i>
      ) in letters to the Hittite kings. He was immediately attacked by Kurigalzu II of Babylon who had been installed by his father, but succeeded in defeating him, repelling Babylonian attempts to invade Assyria, counterattacking and appropriating Babylonian territory in the process, thus further expanding Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      The successor of Enlil-nirari,
      <a href="/wiki/Arik-den-ili" title="Arik-den-ili">
       Arik-den-ili
      </a>
      (c. 13071296 BC), consolidated Assyrian power, and successfully campaigned in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zagros" title="Zagros">
       Zagros
      </a>
      Mountains to the east, subjugating the Lullubi and Gutians. In Syria, he defeated Semitic tribes of the so-called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahlamu" title="Ahlamu">
       Ahlamu
      </a>
      group, who were possibly predecessors of the Arameans or an Aramean tribe.
     </p>
     <p>
      He was followed by
      <a href="/wiki/Adad-nirari_I" title="Adad-nirari I">
       Adad-nirari I
      </a>
      (12951275 BC) who made
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalhu" title="Kalhu">
       Kalhu
      </a>
      (Biblical
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calah" title="Calah">
       Calah
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">
       Nimrud
      </a>
      ) his capital, and continued expansion to the northwest, mainly at the expense of the Hittites and Hurrians, conquering Hittite territories such as
      <a href="/wiki/Carchemish" title="Carchemish">
       Carchemish
      </a>
      and beyond. He then moved into north eastern Asia Minor, conquering
      <a href="/wiki/Shupria" title="Shupria">
       Shupria
      </a>
      . Adad-nirari I made further gains to the south, annexing Babylonian territory and forcing the Kassite rulers of Babylon into accepting a new frontier agreement in Assyria's favour.
     </p>
     <p>
      Adad-nirari's inscriptions are more detailed than any of his predecessors. He declares that the gods of Mesopotamia called him to war, a statement used by most subsequent Assyrian kings. He referred to himself again as
      <i>
       Sharru Rabi
      </i>
      (meaning "The Great King" in the Akkadian language) and conducted extensive building projects in Ashur and the provinces.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1274 BC
      <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_I" title="Shalmaneser I">
       Shalmaneser I
      </a>
      (12741244 BC) ascended the throne. He proved to be a great warrior king. During his reign he conquered the Hurrian kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
       Urartu
      </a>
      that would have encompassed most of Eastern Anatolia and the Caucasus Mountains in the 9th century BC, and the fierce Gutians of the Zagros. He then attacked the Mitanni-Hurrians, defeating both King
      <a href="/wiki/Shattuara" title="Shattuara">
       Shattuara
      </a>
      and his Hittite and Aramaean allies, finally completely destroying the Hurri-Mitanni kingdom in the process.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the campaign against the Hittites, Shattuara cut off the Assyrian army from their supply of food and water, but the Assyrians broke free in a desperate battle, counterattacked, and conquered and annexed what remained of the Mitanni kingdom. Shalmaneser I installed an Assyrian prince,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ilu-ippada&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ilu-ippada (page does not exist)">
       Ilu-ippada
      </a>
      as ruler of Mitanni, with Assyrian governors such as
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Meli-sah&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Meli-sah (page does not exist)">
       Meli-sah
      </a>
      , installed to rule individual cities.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Hittites, having failed to save Mitanni, allied with Babylon in an unsuccessful economic war against Assyria for many years. Assyria was now a large and powerful empire, and a major threat to Egyptian and Hittite interests in the region, and was perhaps the reason that these two powers, fearful of Assyrian might, made peace with one another.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Like his father, Shalmaneser was a great builder and he further expanded the city of Kalhu at the juncture of the Tigris and Zab Rivers.
     </p>
     <p>
      Shalmaneser's son and successor,
      <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">
       Tukulti-Ninurta I
      </a>
      (12441207 BC), won a major victory against the Hittites and their king
      <a href="/wiki/Tudhaliya_IV" title="Tudhaliya IV">
       Tudhaliya IV
      </a>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nihriya" title="Battle of Nihriya">
       Battle of Nihriya
      </a>
      and took thousands of prisoners. He then conquered Babylonia, taking
      <a href="/wiki/Kashtiliash_IV" title="Kashtiliash IV">
       Kashtiliash IV
      </a>
      as a captive and ruled there himself as king for seven years, taking on the old title "King of Sumer and Akkad" first used by Sargon of Akkad. Tukulti-Ninurta I thus became the first Akkadian speaking native Mesopotamian to rule the state of Babylonia, its founders having been foreign Amorites, succeeded by equally foreign Kassites. Tukulti-Ninurta petitioned the god
      <a href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">
       Shamash
      </a>
      before beginning his counter offensive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edwards_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-edwards-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Kashtiliash_IV" title="Kashtiliash IV">
       Kashtiliash IV
      </a>
      was captured, single-handed by Tukulti-Ninurta according to
      <i>
       his
      </i>
      account, who "trod with my feet upon his lordly neck as though it were a footstool"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and deported him ignominiously in chains to Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      The victorious Assyrian demolished the walls of Babylon, massacred many of the inhabitants, pillaged and plundered his way across the city to the
      <a href="/wiki/Esagila" title="Esagila">
       Esagila
      </a>
      temple, where he made off with the statue of
      <a href="/wiki/Marduk" title="Marduk">
       Marduk
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He then proclaimed himself "king of Karduniash, king of Sumer and Akkad, king of
      <a href="/wiki/Sippar" title="Sippar">
       Sippar
      </a>
      and Babylon, king of
      <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">
       Tilmun
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Meluhha" title="Meluhha">
       Meluhha
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edwards_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-edwards-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Middle Assyrian texts recovered at ancient
      <a href="/wiki/D%C5%ABr-Katlimmu" title="Dr-Katlimmu">
       Dr-Katlimmu
      </a>
      , include a letter from Tukulti-Ninurta to his
      <i>
       sukkal rabi'u
      </i>
      , or grand vizier,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ashur-iddin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ashur-iddin (page does not exist)">
       Ashur-iddin
      </a>
      advising him of the approach of his general
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shulman-mushabshu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shulman-mushabshu (page does not exist)">
       Shulman-mushabshu
      </a>
      escorting the captive Kashtiliash, his wife, and his retinue which incorporated a large number of women,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      on his way to exile after his defeat. In the process he defeated the Elamites, who had themselves coveted Babylon. He also wrote an epic poem documenting his wars against Babylon and Elam. After a Babylonian revolt, he raided and plundered the temples in Babylon, regarded as an act of sacrilege. As relations with the priesthood in Ashur began deteriorating, Tukulti-Ninurta built a new capital city;
      <a href="/wiki/Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta" title="Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta">
       Kar-Tukulti-Ninurta
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A number of historians, including
      <a href="/wiki/Julian_Jaynes" title="Julian Jaynes">
       Julian Jaynes
      </a>
      , identify
      <a href="/wiki/Tukulti-Ninurta_I" title="Tukulti-Ninurta I">
       Tukulti-Ninurta I
      </a>
      and his deeds as the historical origin for the fictional biblical character
