      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manneans" title="Manneans">
       Manneans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
       Israelites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
       Phoenicians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
       Urartians
      </a>
      were vanquished and regarded as vassals and Assyria's empire was kept secure.
     </p>
     <p>
      He imposed a so-called
      <i>
       Vassal Treaty
      </i>
      upon his
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">
       Persian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">
       Median
      </a>
      subjects, forcing
      <a href="/wiki/Teispes" title="Teispes">
       Teispes
      </a>
      of Persia and
      <a href="/wiki/Deioces" title="Deioces">
       Deioces
      </a>
      of Media to submit both to himself, and in advance to his chosen successor, Ashurbanipal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Esarhaddon died whilst preparing to leave for Egypt to once more eject the
      <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">
       Nubians
      </a>
      , who were attempting to encroach on the southern part of the country. This task was successfully completed by his successor,
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">
       Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Under
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">
       Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      (669627 BC), Assyrian domination spanned from the
      <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">
       Caucasus Mountains
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
       Armenia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">
       Georgia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">
       Azerbaijan
      </a>
      ) in the north to
      <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">
       Nubia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">
       Libya
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">
       Arabia
      </a>
      in the south, and from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Mediterranean" title="East Mediterranean">
       East Mediterranean
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">
       Antioch
      </a>
      in the west to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persia
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">
       Caspian Sea
      </a>
      in the east.
     </p>
     <p>
      He was an unusually educated man for his time, being able to read and write in Akkadian, Aramaic and Sumerian, and having a proficient understanding of Astronomy and Mathematics, as well as military, civil and political aptitude. He built the famed
      <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Ashurbanipal" title="Library of Ashurbanipal">
       Library of Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      which contained a multitude of ancient texts from all over Mesopotamia, and was the first library in history to classify works in order of genre.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Susa-destruction.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="500" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Susa-destruction.jpg/220px-Susa-destruction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Susa-destruction.jpg/330px-Susa-destruction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Susa-destruction.jpg/440px-Susa-destruction.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Susa-destruction.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Ashurbanipal's brutal campaign against
        <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">
         Elam
        </a>
        in 647 BCE is recorded in this relief.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Ashurbanipal began his rule by easily crushing the
      <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">
       Nubian
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">
       Cushite
      </a>
      king
      <a href="/wiki/Taharqa" title="Taharqa">
       Taharqa
      </a>
      , who had attempted to invade the southern part of Assyrian-controlled Egypt.
      <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">
       Memphis
      </a>
      was sacked, and
      <a href="/wiki/Taharqa" title="Taharqa">
       Taharqa
      </a>
      was chased back into
      <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">
       Nubia
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">
       Sudan
      </a>
      ) by a pursuing Assyrian army, and was never again to pose a threat. Ashurbanipal then put down a series of rebellions by the native Egyptians themselves, installing
      <a href="/wiki/Necho_I" title="Necho I">
       Necho I
      </a>
      as a puppet Pharaoh. However in 664 BC, the new Nubian-Kushite king
      <a href="/wiki/Tantamani" title="Tantamani">
       Tantamani
      </a>
      once more attempted to invade Egypt, however he was savagely crushed,
      <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">
       Thebes
      </a>
      was sacked and looted, and he fled to Nubia, bringing to an end, once and for all, Nubian designs on Egypt.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Phraortes" title="Phraortes">
       Phraortes
      </a>
      , the king of the
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      and Persians, also rebelled against Assyria, and attempted to attack Assyria itself in 653 BC, however he met with defeat at the hands of Ashurbanipal, and was killed. The succeeding Median kings,
      <a href="/wiki/Madius" title="Madius">
       Madius
      </a>
      and then
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyaxares_the_Great" title="Cyaxares the Great">
       Cyaxares the Great
      </a>
      , were both in turn subjugated by Ashurbanipal.
     </p>
     <p>
      At around this time,
      <a href="/wiki/Gyges_of_Lydia" title="Gyges of Lydia">
       Gyges
      </a>
      king of
      <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">
       Lydia
      </a>
      in western Asia Minor, offered his submission to Ashurbanipal.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 652 BC, just one year after his victory over Phraortes, his own brother
      <a href="/wiki/Shamash-shum-ukin" title="Shamash-shum-ukin">
       Shamash-shum-ukin
      </a>
      , the Assyrian king of
      <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
       Babylon
      </a>
      who had spent seventeen years peacefully subject to his sibling, became infused with Babylonian nationalism, declaring that Babylon and not Nineveh should be the seat of empire. Shamash-shum-ukin raised a powerful coalition of peoples resentful of being subject to Assyria, including-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">
       Babylonians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_Dynasty" title="Chaldean Dynasty">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
       Persians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Suteans" title="Suteans">
       Suteans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
       Arabs
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
       Scythians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
       Cimmerians
      </a>
      , and even some
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians" title="Assyrians">
       Assyrians
      </a>
      . War raged between the two brothers for five years, until in 648 BC, Babylon was sacked, and Shamash-shum-ukin slain. Ashurbanipal then wrought savage revenge,
      <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">
       Elam
      </a>
      was utterly destroyed, the Aramean, Chaldean, Sutean tribes were brutally punished,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">
       Arabia
      </a>
      was ravaged by the Assyrian army, and its rebellious shiekhs put to death.
      <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_I" title="Cyrus I">
       Cyrus I
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persia
      </a>
      (grandfather of
      <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">
       Cyrus the Great
      </a>
      ) was forced into submission, as a part of this defeated alliance.
     </p>
     <p>
      Late in his reign, Ashurbanipal was forced to contend with renewed attempts on his empire by the
      <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
       Scythians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
       Cimmerians
      </a>
      . The Scythians were able to once more ravage Assyria's Median and Persian colonies in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" title="Ancient Iran">
       Ancient Iran
      </a>
      before being finally subdued, and the last decade or so of his reign seems to have been peaceful.
     </p>
     <p>
      He built vast libraries and initiated a surge in the building of temples and palaces. After the crushing of the Babylonian revolt, Ashurbanipal appeared master of all he surveyed. To the east,
      <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">
       Elam
      </a>
      was devastated and prostrate before Assyria, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manneans" title="Manneans">
       Manneans
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
       Iranian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persians
      </a>
      and Medes were vassals. To the south,
      <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
       Babylonia
      </a>
      was occupied, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_people" title="Chaldean people">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
       Arabs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sutu,_Iran" title="Sutu, Iran">
       Sutu
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabateans" title="Nabateans">
       Nabateans
      </a>
      subjugated, the
      <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">
       Nubian
      </a>
      empire destroyed, and
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      paid tribute. To the north, the
      <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
       Scythians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
       Cimmerians
      </a>
      had been vanquished and driven from Assyrian territory,
      <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
       Urartu
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
       Armenia
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">
       Phrygia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Corduene" title="Corduene">
       Corduene
      </a>
      and the neo
      <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">
       Hittites
      </a>
      were in vassalage, and
      <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">
       Lydia
      </a>
      pleading for Assyrian protection. To the west,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramea" title="Aramea">
       Aramea
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      ), the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">
       Phoenicians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
       Israel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Judah
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">
       Samarra
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      were subjugated, and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenised" title="Hellenised">
       Hellenised
      </a>
      inhabitants of
      <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">
       Caria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">
       Cilicia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">
       Cappadocia
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commagene" title="Commagene">
       Commagene
      </a>
      paid tribute to Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria conquered the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-fifth dynasty of Egypt">
       25th dynasty Egypt
      </a>
      (expelling its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nubian_people" title="Nubian people">
       Nubian
      </a>
      /
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushite" title="Kushite">
       Kushite
      </a>
      dynasty) as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
       Babylonia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldea
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">
       Elam
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Median_Empire" title="Median Empire">
       Media
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
       Urartu
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
       Armenia
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
       Phoenicia
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramea" title="Aramea">
       Aramea
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">
       Phrygia
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Hittites" title="Neo-Hittites">
       Neo-Hittite States
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurrian" title="Hurrian">
       Hurrian lands
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">
       Arabia
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutium" title="Gutium">
       Gutium
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
       Israel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Judah
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">
       Samarra
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">
       Moab
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">
       Edom
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Corduene" title="Corduene">
       Corduene
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">
       Cilicia
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mannea" title="Mannea">
       Mannea
      </a>
      and parts of
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
       Ancient Greece
      </a>
      (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      ), and defeated and/or exacted tribute from
      <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">
       Scythia
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean_Peninsula" title="Crimean Peninsula">
       Cimmeria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">
       Lydia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">
       Nubia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">
       Ethiopia
      </a>
      and others.
     </p>
     <p>
      At its height, the Empire encompassed the whole of the modern nations of
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
       Iraq
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">
       Lebanon
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
       Israel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">
       Jordan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">
       Kuwait
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">
       Bahrain
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      , together with large swathes of
      <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
       Iran
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">
       Saudi Arabia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
       Turkey
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">
       Sudan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">
       Libya
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
       Armenia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">
       Georgia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">
       Azerbaijan
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria now appeared stronger than ever. However, the long struggles pacifying the Babylonians, Chaldeans, Arameans and Elamites, the exertions undertaken in keeping the Medes, Scythians, Persians, Urartians and Cimmerians subjugated, and the constant campaigning over three centuries to control and expand its vast empire in all directions, had left Assyria exhausted.
     </p>
     <p>
      It had been drained of wealth and manpower; the devastated provinces could yield nothing to supply the needs of the imperial exchequer, it was difficult to find sufficient troops to garrison and effectively control the huge empire, and after the death of Ashurbanipal severe civil unrest broke out in Assyria itself, and the empire began to unravel.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Downfall.2C_626.E2.80.93605_BC">
       Downfall, 626605 BC
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Downfall, 626605 BC">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Assyrian Empire was severely crippled following the death of Ashurbanipal in 627 BCthe nation and its empire descending into a prolonged and brutal series of civil wars involving three rival kings,
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-etil-ilani" title="Ashur-etil-ilani">
       Ashur-etil-ilani
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sin-shumu-lishir" title="Sin-shumu-lishir">
       Sin-shumu-lishir
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sin-shar-ishkun" title="Sin-shar-ishkun">
       Sin-shar-ishkun
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      's 26th Dynasty, which had been installed by the Assyrians as vassals, quietly detached itself from Assyria, although it was careful to retain friendly relations.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-etil-ilani" title="Ashur-etil-ilani">
       Ashur-etil-ilani
      </a>
      came to the throne in 626 BC, and was immediately beset by a series of internal civil wars. He was deposed in 623 BC, after four years of bitter fighting by
      <a href="/wiki/Sin-shumu-lishir" title="Sin-shumu-lishir">
       Sin-shumu-lishir
      </a>
      , an Assyrian
      <i>
       Turtanu
      </i>
      (General) who also occupied and claimed the throne of
      <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
       Babylon
      </a>
      in that year. In turn, Sin-shumu-lishir was deposed as ruler of Assyria and Babylonia after a year of warfare by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sin-shar-ishkun" title="Sin-shar-ishkun">
       Sin-shar-ishkun
      </a>
      (622612 BC)who was then himself faced with constant violent rebellion in the Assyrian homeland.
     </p>
     <p>
      This situation led to wholesale revolution in
      <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
       Babylonia
      </a>
      , and during the reign of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sin-shar-ishkun" title="Sin-shar-ishkun">
       Sin-shar-ishkun
      </a>
      many Assyrian colonies to the west, east and north similarly took advantage and ceased to pay tribute to Assyria, most significantly the
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
       Scythians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
       Cimmerians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">
       Babylonians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The Scythians and Cimmerians took advantage of the bitter fighting among the Assyrians to raid Assyrian colonies, with hordes of horse borne marauders ravaging parts of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
       Asia Minor
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">
       Caucasus
      </a>
      , where the vassal kings of
      <a href="/wiki/Urartu" title="Urartu">
       Urartu
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">
       Lydia
      </a>
      begged their Assyrian overlord for help in vain. They also raided the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(Samaria)" title="Kingdom of Israel (Samaria)">
       Israel
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Judah
      </a>
      (where
      <a href="/wiki/Ashkelon" title="Ashkelon">
       Ashkelon
      </a>
      was sacked by the Scythians) and all the way into
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      whose coasts were ravaged and looted with impunity.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranic" title="Iranic">
       Iranic
      </a>
      peoples (the
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persians
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthians" title="Parthians">
       Parthians
      </a>
      ), aided by the previous Assyrian destruction of the hitherto dominant
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Iran" title="Ancient Iran">
       Ancient Iran
      </a>
      , also took advantage of the upheavals in Assyria to coalesce into a powerful Median dominated force which destroyed the
      <i>
       pre-Iranic
      </i>
      Assyrian vassal kingdom of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mannea" title="Mannea">
       Mannea
      </a>
      and absorbed the remnants of the pre-Iranic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      of southern Iran, and the equally pre-Iranic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutians" title="Gutians">
       Gutians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manneans" title="Manneans">
       Manneans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
       Kassites
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">
       Zagros Mountains
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">
       Caspian Sea
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Aram_(biblical_region)" title="Aram (biblical region)">
       Aram
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">
       Phoenicia
      </a>
      and southern
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaan
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
       Israel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">
       Jordan
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinai" title="Sinai">
       Sinai
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      ), the various Aramean, Phoenician and Jewish states quietly reasserted their independence, and in western Asia Minor and eastern
      <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_sea_(oceanography)" title="Mediterranean sea (oceanography)">
       Mediterranean
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Lydians" title="Lydians">
       Lydians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
       Greeks
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilicians" title="Cilicians">
       Cilicians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Carians" title="Carians">
       Carians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">
       Cappadocian
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luwian" title="Luwian">
       Luwian
      </a>
      states did the same.
      <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">
       Armenians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">
       Sarmatians
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colchians" title="Colchians">
       Colchians
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Georgians" title="Georgians">
       Georgians
      </a>
      ) also began to establish themselves in parts of the Caucasus.
     </p>
     <p>
      By 620 BC,
      <a href="/wiki/Nabopolassar" title="Nabopolassar">
       Nabopolassar
      </a>
      , (a previously unknown
      <i>
       Malka
      </i>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldean
      </a>
      tribes who had settled the far southeast of
      <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">
       Mesopotamia
      </a>
      circa 900 BC) had claimed the city of Babylon and swathes of Babylonia in the confusion. Sin-shar-ishkun amassed a large army to eject Nabopolassar from Babylon; however, yet another massive revolt broke out in Assyria proper, forcing the bulk of his army to turn back, where they promptly joined the rebels in
      <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
       Nineveh
      </a>
      . Similarly, Nabopolassar was unable to gain control over all of Babylonia, and could not make any inroads into Assyria despite its weakened state, being repelled at every attempt. The next four years saw bitter fighting in the heart of Babylonia itself, as the Assyrians tried to wrest back control.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_2-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, in 615 BC Nabopolassar entered into an alliance with the Median king
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyaxares_the_Great" title="Cyaxares the Great">
       Cyaxares the Great
      </a>
      , a hitherto vassal of Assyria, who had taken advantage of the upheavals in Assyria to free the Iranian peoples from Assyrian vassalage and unite the
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
       Iranian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persians
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthians" title="Parthians">
       Parthians
      </a>
      , together with the remnants of the pre-Iranian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutians" title="Gutians">
       Gutians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
       Kassites
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manneans" title="Manneans">
       Manneans
      </a>
      , into a powerful Median-dominated force.
     </p>
     <p>
      Mass alliances against Assyria were not a new phenomenon. During the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">
       Middle Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      (1365-1020 BC), peoples such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">
       Hittites
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">
       Babylonians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">
       Mitanni
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">
       Hurrians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">
       Phrygians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kassites" title="Kassites">
       Kassites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gutians" title="Gutians">
       Gutians
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canaanites" title="Canaanites">
       Canaanites
      </a>
      had formed various coalitions at different times in vain attempts to break Assyrian power. During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo_Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo Assyrian Empire">
       Neo Assyrian Empire
      </a>
      , in the reigns of
      <a href="/wiki/Shalmaneser_III" title="Shalmaneser III">
       Shalmaneser III
      </a>
      in the 9th century BC,
      <a href="/wiki/Sargon_II" title="Sargon II">
       Sargon II
      </a>
      in the 8th century BC, and
      <a href="/wiki/Sennacherib" title="Sennacherib">
       Sennacherib
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">
       Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      in the earlier part of the 7th century BC, combined attempts to break Assyrian dominance by alliances including at different times;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">
       Babylonians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">
       Egyptians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
       Greeks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
       Persians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elamites" title="Elamites">
       Elamites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nubians" title="Nubians">
       Nubians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_Dynasty" title="Chaldean Dynasty">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">
       Phoenicians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canaanites" title="Canaanites">
       Canaanites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lydians" title="Lydians">
       Lydians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Suteans" title="Suteans">
       Suteans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
       Israelites
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judean" title="Judean">
       Judeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
       Scythians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
       Cimmerians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manneans" title="Manneans">
       Manneans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urartians" title="Urartians">
       Urartians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilicians" title="Cilicians">
       Cilicians
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Hittites" title="Neo-Hittites">
       Neo-Hittites
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
       Arabs
      </a>
      had all failed, Assyria being strong, well led and united, at the height of its power, and able to deal with any threat.
     </p>
     <p>
      However the nation at this time was in a severely depleted state, ravaged by over a decade of internal civil war, disunity, instability, economic crisis and battle fatigue, and the forces ranged against it from all sides were to prove too much for the severely weakened Assyrians.
     </p>
     <p>
      While Sin-shar-ishkun was fighting both the home grown rebels in Assyria and the Chaldeans and Babylonians in southern Mesopotamia,
      <a href="/wiki/Cyaxares" title="Cyaxares">
       Cyaxares
      </a>
      (technically a vassal of Assyria), in an alliance with the Scythians and Cimmerians, launched a surprise attack on a civil war bleaguered Assyria in 615 BC, sacking Kalhu (the Biblical Calah/Nimrud) and taking Arrapkha (modern Kirkuk). Nabopolassar, still pinned down in southern Mesopotamia, was completely uninvolved in this major breakthrough against Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, from this point, the alliance of
      <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">
       Medes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
       Persians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonians" title="Babylonians">
       Babylonians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">
       Scythians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">
       Cimmerians
      </a>
      fought in unison against Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite the sorely depleted state of Assyria, bitter fighting ensued; throughout 614 BC the alliance of powers continued to gradually make hard fought inroads into Assyria itself, however in 613 BC the Assyrians somehow rallied against the odds and scored a number of counterattacking victories over the Medes-Persians, Babylonians-Chaldeans and Scythians-Cimmerians.
     </p>
     <p>
      This led to the coalition of forces ranged against it to unite and launch a massive combined attack in 612 BC, finally besieging and entering Nineveh in late 612 BC, with Sin-shar-ishkun being slain in the bitter street by street fighting.
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite the loss of almost all of its major cities, and in the face of overwhelming odds, Assyrian resistance continued.
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_II" title="Ashur-uballit II">
       Ashur-uballit II
      </a>
      (612- 605 BC) took the throne amid the street by street fighting in Nineveh, and refused a request to bow in vassalage to Nabopolassar, Cyaxares and their allies. He managed to break out of Nineveh and successfully fight his way to the northern Assyrian city of
      <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">
       Harran
      </a>
      , he took the city and founded it as a new capital. Ashur-uballit II somehow managed to keep control of a now greatly reduced Assyria for five years or so, repelling attacks by his enemies. However, Harran too was eventually besieged and taken by the Medes, Babylonians and Scythians in 608 BC, with Ashur-uballit II once more managing to break free of the siege.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      , itself a former Assyrian colony whose current dynasty had been installed as puppet rulers by the Assyrians, then came to the aid of its former master, possibly in fear that without Assyrian protection it would be next to succumb, having already been ravaged by the Scythians.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_2-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_II" title="Ashur-uballit II">
       Ashur-uballit II
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Necho_II" title="Necho II">
       Necho
      </a>
      of Egypt made a failed attempt to recapture Harran in 608 BC. The next three years saw the remnants of the Assyrian army and their Egyptian allies vainly attempting to eject the invaders from Assyria. In 605 BC, the Babylonians, Scythians and Medes-Persians defeated the Assyrians and Egyptians at
      <a href="/wiki/Carchemish" title="Carchemish">
       Carchemish
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Sections of the Assyrian army retreated to the western corner of Assyria after the fall of Harran and Carchemish, and a number of Assyrian imperial records survive between 604 BC and 599 BC in and around the Assyrian city of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dur-Katlimmu" title="Dur-Katlimmu">
       Dur-Katlimmu
      </a>
      in what is today north eastern Syria, and so it is possible that remnants of the Assyrian administration and army still continued to hold out in the region for a few years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The fate of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur-uballit_II" title="Ashur-uballit II">
       Ashur-uballit II
      </a>
      remains unknown, his
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Limmu_Lists&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Limmu Lists (page does not exist)">
       Limmu Lists
      </a>
      end after the fall of Harran, and it is possible he was either killed at this time, at the battle of Carshemish in 605 BC, or continued to fight on, eventually simply disappearing into obscurity.
     </p>
     <p>
      Certainly by 599 BC at the very latest, Assyria had been destroyed as an independent political entity, although it was to launch major rebellions against the
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
       Achaemenid Empire
      </a>
      in 546 BC and 520 BC, and remained a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geo-political" title="Geo-political">
       geo-political
      </a>
      region, ethnic entity and colonised province until the late 7th century AD, with small Assyrian states emerging in the region between the 2nd century BC and 4th century AD.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Society">
       Society
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Society">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Assyrian_Period">
       Middle Assyrian Period
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Middle Assyrian Period">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_troops_return.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="942" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Assyrian_troops_return.png/220px-Assyrian_troops_return.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Assyrian_troops_return.png/330px-Assyrian_troops_return.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Assyrian_troops_return.png/440px-Assyrian_troops_return.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_troops_return.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Assyrian troops return after victory.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Middle Assyrian Period was marked by the long wars fought that helped build Assyria into a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warrior_society" title="Warrior society">
       warrior society
      </a>
      . The king depended on both the citizen class and priests in his capital, and the landed nobility who supplied the horses needed by Assyria's military. Documents and letters illustrate the importance of the latter to Assyrian society.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria needed less artificial
      <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">
       irrigation
      </a>
      than Babylon, and horse-breeding was extensive. Portions of elaborate texts about the care and training of them have been found. Trade was carried out in all directions. The mountain country to the north and west of Assyria was a major source of metal ore, as well as lumber. Economic factors were a common
      <i>
       casus belli
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Law">
       Law
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Law">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flaying_of_rebels.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="1312" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Flaying_of_rebels.jpg/220px-Flaying_of_rebels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Flaying_of_rebels.jpg/330px-Flaying_of_rebels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Flaying_of_rebels.jpg/440px-Flaying_of_rebels.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flaying_of_rebels.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Assyrians skinning or
        <a href="/wiki/Flaying" title="Flaying">
         flaying
        </a>
        their prisoners alive
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      All free male citizens were obliged to serve in the army for a time, a system which was called the
      <i>
       ilku
      </i>
      -service. A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_code" title="Legal code">
       legal code
      </a>
      was produced during the 14th and 13th centuries which, among other things, clearly shows that the social position of women in Assyria was lower than that of neighbouring societies. Men were permitted to
      <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">
       divorce
      </a>
      their wives with no compensation paid to the latter. If a woman committed
      <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">
       adultery
      </a>
      , she could be beaten or put to death. It's not certain if these laws were seriously enforced, but they appear to be a backlash against some older documents that granted things like equal compensation to both partners in divorce.
     </p>
     <p>
      The women of the king's harem and their servants were also subject to harsh punishments, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Strike_(attack)" title="Strike (attack)">
       beatings
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mutilation" title="Mutilation">
       mutilation
      </a>
      , and death. Assyria, in general, had much harsher laws than most of the region.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Execution" title="Execution">
       Executions
      </a>
      were not uncommon, nor were whippings followed by forced labour. Some offenses allowed the accused a trial under torture/duress. One tablet that covers property rights has brutal penalties for violators. A creditor could force debtors to work for him, but not sell them.
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria, like much of Mesopotamia, was initially open to homosexual relationships between men. Male prostitutes and eunuchs could enjoy fame and influence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dynes_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dynes-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the Middle Assyrian Laws, sex crimes were punished identically whether they were homosexual or heterosexual.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dynes_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dynes-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nissinen_50-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nissinen-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, homosexual relationships between social equals, or those where a social better was submissive or penetrated, were treated as rape. However, an individual with a higher social class faced no punishment for penetrating someone of a lower social class, or devotees of Ishtar (whose gender roles were not considered solidly masculine). Omen texts referred to male homosexual acts without moral judgement or affirmation, though social distinctions determined whether the omen was considered beneficial or malevolent. In some ways, these omens conflicted with the law codes, though the law may have been enacted to curtail the omen's power.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nissinen_50-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nissinen-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One historian notes that the laws would not be so detailed "if homosexual behavior were not a familiar aspect of daily life of early Mesopotamia."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">
       Architecture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Architecture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Assyrian architecture, like that of Babylonia, was influenced by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumero-Akkadian" title="Sumero-Akkadian">
       Sumero-Akkadian
      </a>
      styles (and to some degree Mitanni), but early on developed its own distinctive style. Palaces sported colourful wall decorations, and seal-cutting (an art learned from Mittani) developed apace. Schools for scribes taught both the Babylonian and Assyrian dialects of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian" title="Akkadian">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">
       Sumerian
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian" title="Akkadian">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      literary works were often copied with an Assyrian flavour.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrian dialect of Akkadian was used in legal, official, religious, and practical texts such as medicine or instructions on manufacturing items. During the 13th to 10th centuries, picture tales appeared as a new art form: a continuous series of images carved on square stone steles. Somewhat reminiscent of a comic book, these show events such as warfare or hunting, placed in order from the upper left to the lower right corner of the stele with captions written underneath them. These and the excellent cut seals show that Assyrian art was beginning to surpass that of Babylon. Architecture saw the introduction of a new style of
      <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat" title="Ziggurat">
       ziggurat
      </a>
      , with two towers and colorful enameled tiles.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_after_the_empire">
       Assyria after the empire
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Assyria after the empire">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">
       Achaemenid Assyria
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_Assyria.2C_Athura.2C_Assuristan.2C_Assyria_province.2C_Adiabene.2C_Osroene_and_Hatra">
       Achaemenid Assyria, Athura, Assuristan, Assyria province, Adiabene, Osroene and Hatra
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Achaemenid Assyria, Athura, Assuristan, Assyria province, Adiabene, Osroene and Hatra">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Assyria was initially ruled by the short lived
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Median_Empire" title="Median Empire">
       Median Empire
      </a>
      (605-549 BC) after its fall. In a twist of fate,
      <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">
       Nabonidus
      </a>
      the last king of Babylon (together with his son and co-regent
      <a href="/wiki/Belshazzar" title="Belshazzar">
       Belshazzar
      </a>
      ) was himself an Assyrian from
      <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">
       Harran
      </a>
      . He had overthrown the short lived
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldean
      </a>
      dynasty in Babylonia, after which the Chaldeans disappeared from history, being fully absorbed into the native population of Babylonia. However, apart from plans to dedicate religious temples in the city of Harran, Nabonidus showed little interest in rebuilding Assyria.
      <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
       Nineveh
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalhu" title="Kalhu">
       Kalhu
      </a>
      remained in ruins with only small numbers of Assyrians living within them, conversely a number of towns and cities such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrapkha" title="Arrapkha">
       Arrapkha
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guzana" title="Guzana">
       Guzana
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nohadra" title="Nohadra">
       Nohadra
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">
       Harran
      </a>
      remained intact, and
      <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
       Assur
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arbil" title="Arbil">
       Arbela
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irbil" title="Irbil">
       Irbil
      </a>
      ) were not completely destroyed, as is attested by their later revival. However, Assyria spent much of this short period in a degree of devastation following its fall.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_Assyria_.28549.E2.80.93330_BC.29">
       <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">
        Achaemenid Assyria
       </a>
       (549330 BC)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Achaemenid Assyria (549330 BC)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      After the Medes were overthrown by the
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
       Persians
      </a>
      as the dominant force in Ancient Iran, Assyria was ruled by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">
       Achaemenid Empire
      </a>
      (as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athura" title="Athura">
       Athura
      </a>
      ) from 549 BC to 330 BC (see
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">
       Achaemenid Assyria
      </a>
      ). Between 546 and 545 BC, Assyria rebelled against the new Persian Dynasty, which had usurped the previous Median dynasty. The rebellion centered around
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyareh" title="Tyareh">
       Tyareh
      </a>
      was eventually quashed by
      <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">
       Cyrus the Great
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyria seems to have recovered dramatically, and flourished during this period. It became a major agricultural and administrative centre of the Achaemenid Empire, and its soldiers were a mainstay of the Persian Army.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In fact, Assyria even became powerful enough to raise another full-scale revolt against the Persian empire in 520519 BC.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Persians had spent centuries under Assyrian domination (their first ruler
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenes" title="Achaemenes">
       Achaemenes
      </a>
      and his successors, having been vassals of Assyria), and Assyrian influence can be seen in Achaemenid art, infrastructure and administration. Early Persian rulers saw themselves as successors to
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurbanipal" title="Ashurbanipal">
       Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" title="Mesopotamian">
       Mesopotamian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">
       Aramaic
      </a>
      was retained as the
      <i>
       lingua franca
      </i>
      of the empire for over two hundred years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
       Nineveh
      </a>
      was never rebuilt however, and 200 years after it was sacked
      <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">
       Xenophon
      </a>
      reported only small numbers of Assyrians living amongst its ruins. Conversely the ancient city of
      <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
       Assur
      </a>
      once more became a rich and prosperous entity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It was in 5th century BC Assyria that the
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">
       Syriac language
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_script" title="Syriac script">
       Syriac script
      </a>
      evolved. Five centuries later these were later to have a global influence as the liturgical language and written script for
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
       Syriac Christianity
      </a>
      and its accompanying
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">
       Syriac literature
      </a>
      which also emerged in Assyria before spreading throughout the Near East, Asia Minor, The Caucasus, Central Asia, the Indian Subcontinent and China.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Macedonian_and_Seleucid_Assyria">
       Macedonian and Seleucid Assyria
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Macedonian and Seleucid Assyria">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 332 BC, Assyria fell to
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">
       Alexander the Great
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">
       Macedonian
      </a>
      Emperor from
      <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">
       Greece
      </a>
      , who called the inhabitants
      <i>
       Assyrioi
      </i>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_Empire" title="Macedonian Empire">
       Macedonian Empire
      </a>
      (332-312) was partitioned in 312 BC. It thereafter became part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">
       Seleucid Empire
      </a>
      (312 BC) and was renamed
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      , a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurrian" title="Hurrian">
       Hurrian
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luwian" title="Luwian">
       Luwian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      corruption of
      <b>
       Assyria
      </b>
      , a term which for many centuries until the Seleucid era had only and specifically meant and referred to Assyria and the Assyrians, and not to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Levant" title="The Levant">
       The Levant
      </a>
      and its largely Aramean, Phoenician and Neo-Hittite inhabitants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is from this period that the later
      <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Syria" title="Name of Syria">
       <i>
        Syria
       </i>
       Vs
       <i>
        Assyria
       </i>
       naming controversy arises
      </a>
      , the Seleucids applied the name not only to Assyria itself, but also to the Levantine lands to the west (historically known as
      <a href="/wiki/Aram_(biblical_region)" title="Aram (biblical region)">
       Aram
      </a>
      modern
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      /
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eber_Nari" title="Eber Nari">
       Eber Nari
      </a>
      ), which had been part of the Assyrian empire but never a part of Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      When they lost control of Assyria, the name
      <i>
       Syria
      </i>
      survived but was erroneously applied only to the land of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramea" title="Aramea">
       Aramea
      </a>
      (also known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eber_Nari" title="Eber Nari">
       Eber Nari
      </a>
      ) to the west that had once been part of the Assyrian empire, but apart from the north eastern corner, had never been a part of Assyria itself, nor inhabited by Assyrians. This was to lead to both the Assyrians from Northern Mesopotamia and the Arameans and Phoenicians from the Levant being collectively dubbed
      <b>
       Syrians
      </b>
      (and later also
      <b>
       Syriacs
      </b>
      ) in
      <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">
       Greco-Roman
      </a>
      culture, regardless of ethnicity, history or geography.
     </p>
     <p>
      During Seleucid rule, Assyrians ceased to hold the senior military and civil positions they had enjoyed under the Achaemenids, being largely replaced by Greeks. The Greek language also replaced Mesopotamian East Aramaic as the lingua franca of the empire, although this did not affect the Assyrian population themselves, who were not Hellenised during the Seleucid era.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the Seleucid period in southern Mesopotamia,
      <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">
       Babylon
      </a>
      was gradually abandoned in favour of a new city named
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dura_Seleucus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dura Seleucus (page does not exist)">
       Dura Seleucus
      </a>
      , effectively bringing an end to
      <i>
       Babylonia
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parthian_Assyria_.28150_BC_.E2.80.93_116_AD.29.3B_Adiabene_.2869_BC_.E2.80.93_117_AD.29">
       Parthian Assyria (150 BC  116 AD);
       <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">
        Adiabene
       </a>
       (69 BC  117 AD)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Parthian Assyria (150 BC  116 AD); Adiabene (69 BC  117 AD)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      By 150 BC, Assyria was largely under the control of the
      <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">
       Parthian Empire
      </a>
      , once more as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athura" title="Athura">
       Athura
      </a>
      (the Mesopotamian East Aramaic word for Assyria). The Parthians seem to have exercised only loose control over Assyria. Temples to the native gods of Assyria were resurrected in many towns and cities. A number of independent
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian" title="Neo-Assyrian">
       Neo-Assyrian
      </a>
      states arose, the most notable being
      <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">
       Adiabene
      </a>
      (69 BC - 117 AD). Adiabene was described by historian
      <a href="/wiki/Georges_Roux" title="Georges Roux">
       Georges Roux
      </a>
      as a virtual resurrection of Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrians began to convert to
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_religion" title="Mesopotamian religion">
       Mesopotamian religion
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Ashurism" title="Ashurism">
       Ashurism
      </a>
      ) during the period between the early 1st and 3rd centuries AD, with the founding of the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
       Assyrian Church of the East
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Assyria_.28116_AD_.E2.80.93_118_AD.29">
       Roman Assyria (116 AD  118 AD)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Roman Assyria (116 AD  118 AD)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      However, in 116 AD, under
      <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">
       Trajan
      </a>
      , Assyria and its independent states were taken over by Rome as the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(Roman_province)" title="Assyria (Roman province)">
       Roman Province of Assyria
      </a>
      . The Assyrian kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">
       Adiabene
      </a>
      was destroyed as an independent state during this period. Roman rule lasted only a few years, and the Parthians once more regained control with the help of the Assyrians, who were incited to overthrow the Roman garrisons by the Parthian king. However, a number of Assyrians were conscripted into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Army" title="Roman Army">
       Roman Army
      </a>
      , with many serving in the region of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrians_Wall" title="Hadrians Wall">
       Hadrians Wall
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Britain" title="Ancient Britain">
       Ancient Britain
      </a>
      , and inscriptions in Aramaic made by
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramean" title="Aramean">
       Aramean
      </a>
      soldiers have been discovered in northern
      <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
       England
      </a>
      dating from the 2nd century AD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parthian_Assyria_restored_.28119_AD_.E2.80.93_225_AD.29.2C_Osroene.2C_Hatra">
       Parthian Assyria restored (119 AD  225 AD),
       <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">
        Osroene
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">
        Hatra
       </a>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Parthian Assyria restored (119 AD  225 AD), Osroene, Hatra">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
       Romans
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthians" title="Parthians">
       Parthians
      </a>
      fought over Assyria and the rest of Mesopotamia for the next century, allowing a number of other Neo-Assyrian states to arise, namely
      <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">
       Osroene
      </a>
      (132 BC to AD 244) and
      <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">
       Hatra
      </a>
      (155241 AD). Osroene became the first
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christian
      </a>
      state in history, and a major center of
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">
       Syriac literature
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
       Syriac Christianity
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition, the ancient capital city of
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      again flourished, and appears to have gained independence during the 2nd and 3rd centuries AD. Temples to the Assyrian national gods
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">
       Sin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">
       Hadad
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" title="Ishtar">
       Ishtar
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">
       Shamash
      </a>
      were once more dedicated throughout Assyria during this period. The noted Assyriologist
      <a href="/wiki/Simo_Parpola" title="Simo Parpola">
       Simo Parpola
      </a>
      has speculated that Assyria may well have once again been fully independent for a while.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sassanid_Assyria_.28Assuristan_.28226_AD_.E2.80.93_circa_650_AD.29">
       Sassanid Assyria (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assuristan" title="Assuristan">
        Assuristan
       </a>
       (226 AD  circa 650 AD)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Sassanid Assyria (Assuristan (226 AD  circa 650 AD)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 226 AD, Assyria was largely taken over by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">
       Sassanid Empire
      </a>
      . After driving out the Romans and Parthians, the Sassanid rulers set about destroying the independent states within Assyria; Hatra was dissolved in 241 AD, Osroene in 244 AD and Assur was sacked by
      <a href="/wiki/Shapur_I" title="Shapur I">
       Shapur I
      </a>
      in 256 AD.
     </p>
     <p>
      It was known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asuristan" title="Asuristan">
       Asuristan
      </a>
      (the Sassanid name for Assyria) during this period, and became the birthplace of the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">
       Church of the East
      </a>
      (now split into the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
       Assyrian Church of the East
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
       Chaldean Catholic Church
      </a>
      ), with a flourishing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac" title="Syriac">
       Syriac
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      ) Christian culture which exists there to this day. Temples were still being dedicated to the national god
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">
       Ashur
      </a>
      in his home city and in
      <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">
       Harran
      </a>
      during the 4th century, indicating an Assyrian identity was still strong.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the Sassanid period, much of what had once been
      <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">
       Babylonia
      </a>
      in southern Mesopotamia was incorporated into Assyria.
     </p>
     <p>
      Parts of Assyria appear to have been semi independent as late as the latter part of the 4th century AD, with a king named
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sennacherib_II" title="Sennacherib II">
       Sennacherib II
      </a>
      ruling the northern reaches in 370s AD.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrians_after_Assyria">
       Assyrians after Assyria
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Assyrians after Assyria">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_continuity" title="Assyrian continuity">
       Assyrian continuity
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_People" title="Assyrian People">
       Assyrian People
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Assyria.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="341" data-file-width="512" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Flag_of_Assyria.svg/220px-Flag_of_Assyria.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Flag_of_Assyria.svg/330px-Flag_of_Assyria.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Flag_of_Assyria.svg/440px-Flag_of_Assyria.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Assyria.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_flag" title="Assyrian flag">
         Assyrian flag
        </a>
        (since 1968)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
         <a href="#cite_note-57">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          56
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyria_Map.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="377" data-file-width="351" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Assyria_Map.jpg/220px-Assyria_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Assyria_Map.jpg/330px-Assyria_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/Assyria_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyria_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Map of Assyrian populated areas
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Centuries of constant warfare between the
      <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
       Byzantine Empire
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">
       Sassanid Empire
      </a>
      left both empires exhausted, depleted and battle-fatigued, allowing the Muslim Arabs to break from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">
       Arabian peninsula
      </a>
      and invade territories hitherto held by these empires. After the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
       Arab
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic">
       Islamic
      </a>
      conquest in the 7th century, Assyria was dissolved as an entity, although the native Assyrian population still regarded the region as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athura" title="Athura">
       Athura
      </a>
      , an appellation which survived until the 19th century AD. Under Arab rule, Mesopotamia as a whole underwent a gradual process of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabisation" title="Arabisation">
       Arabisation
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamification" title="Islamification">
       Islamification
      </a>
      , and the region saw a gradual large influx of
      <i>
       non indigenous
      </i>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_people" title="Arab people">
       Arabs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
       Kurds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">
       Iranian
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">
       Turkic peoples
      </a>
      . However, the indigenous
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      population of northern Mesopotamia (known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ashuriyun" title="Ashuriyun">
       Ashuriyun
      </a>
      by the Arabs) resisted this process, retaining their language, religion, culture and identity.
     </p>
     <p>
      The previously basic civilisation of the desert dwelling Arabs was greatly enhanced and enriched by the influence and knowledge of native Mesopotamian scientists, physicians, mathematicians, theologians, astronomers, architects, agriculturalists, artists and astrologers.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christianity.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="1273" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/220px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/330px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Syriac_Christianity.svg/440px-Syriac_Christianity.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syriac_Christianity.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Aramaic language and
        <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">
         Syriac Christianity
        </a>
        in the Middle East and Central Asia until being largely annihilated by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamerlane" title="Tamerlane">
         Tamerlane
        </a>
        in the 14th century.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Under the Arab Islamic empires, the Assyrians being Christians were classed as
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhimmis" title="Dhimmis">
        dhimmis
       </a>
      </i>
