      , a kind of second-class citizens who had certain restrictions imposed upon them however had their rights fully protected and were granted freedom in some matters. They were excluded from specific duties and occupations reserved for Muslims, did not enjoy the same political rights as Muslims, their word was not equal to that of a Muslim in legal and civil matters without a Muslim witness, as Christians they were subject to payment of a special tax (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jizyah" title="Jizyah">
       jizyah
      </a>
      ), they were banned from spreading their religion further in Muslim ruled lands, men were banned from marrying Muslim women, but at the same time they were also expected to adhere to the same laws of property, contract and obligation as the Muslim Arabs. They were however also allowed freedom in other matters. Personal matters such as marriage and divorce were governed by the personal law of Assyrians, the internal discipline was ensured by religious leaders who were answerable to the Muslims, they were allowed the consumption of alcohol, were punished equally for a crime as the Muslims and had protection from any agression.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although predominantly Christian, a minority of Assyrians still held onto their ancient Mesopotamian religion until as late as the 10th century AD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineveh.com_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nineveh.com-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Assyrian people, still retaining
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      infused and influenced
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Aramaic" title="Eastern Aramaic">
       Eastern Aramaic
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
       Assyrian Church of the East
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      , remained dominant in the north of Mesopotamia (what had been Assyria) as late as the 14th century AD
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the city of
      <a href="/wiki/Assur" title="Assur">
       Assur
      </a>
      was still occupied by Assyrians during the Islamic period until the mid-14th century when the
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslim
      </a>
      Turco-Mongol ruler
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamurlane" title="Tamurlane">
       Tamurlane
      </a>
      conducted a religiously motivated massacre of indigenous
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">
       Christians
      </a>
      . After that, there are no traces of a settlement at Ashur in the archaeological and numismatic record, and from this point the Assyrian population was dramatically reduced in their homeland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A religious
      <a href="/wiki/Schism" title="Schism">
       schism
      </a>
      among the Assyrians of northern Mesopotamia emerged in the 16th and 17th centuries AD, when a large number of hitherto
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
       Assyrian Church of the East
      </a>
      Assyrians from the far north of Mesopotamia entered communion with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
       Roman Catholic Church
      </a>
      after becoming dissatisfied with the
      <a href="/wiki/Abuna" title="Abuna">
       Abuna
      </a>
      family's leadership of the Assyrian Church, and after at first failing to gain acceptance within the
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">
       Syriac Orthodox Church
      </a>
      . Rome named this new church
      <i>
       The Church of Assyria and Mosul
      </i>
      and its first leader
      <i>
       Patriarch of the East Assyrians
      </i>
      in 1553 AD. However in 1683 AD, Rome altered the name to the
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
       Chaldean Catholic Church
      </a>
      , this group of Assyrians eventually became known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholics" title="Chaldean Catholics">
       Chaldean Catholics
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaldo-Assyrians" title="Chaldo-Assyrians">
       Chaldo-Assyrians
      </a>
      despite having no ethnic, historical, linguistic or geographic connections whatsoever to the long extinct Chaldean tribe of south east Mesopotamia.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrians suffered a number of religiously and ethnically motivated massacres throughout the 17th, 18th and 19th centuries AD,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      culminating in the large scale
      <a href="/wiki/Hamidian_massacres" title="Hamidian massacres">
       Hamidian massacres
      </a>
      of unarmed men, women and children by
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslim
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">
       Turks
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
       Kurds
      </a>
      in the late 19th century at the hands of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
       Ottoman Empire
      </a>
      and its associated (largely
      <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
       Kurdish
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
       Arab
      </a>
      ) militias, which further greatly reduced numbers, particularly in southeastern Turkey.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="540" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/220px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/330px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg/440px-Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_Assyrians.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The burning of bodies of Christian Assyrian women
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Assyrians suffered a further catastrophic series of events during
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      in the form of the religiously and ethnically motivated
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Genocide" title="Assyrian Genocide">
       Assyrian Genocide
      </a>
      at the hands of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottomans" title="Ottomans">
       Ottomans
      </a>
      and their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">
       Kurdish
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
       Arab
      </a>
      allies from 19151918.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some sources claim that the highest number of Assyrians killed during the period was 750,000, while a 1922 Assyrian assessment set it at 275,000. The Assyrian Genocide ran largely in conjunction to the similarly motivated
      <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">
       Armenian Genocide
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_Genocide" title="Greek Genocide">
       Greek Genocide
      </a>
      . In reaction against Turkish cruelty, Assyrians took up arms, and an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_war_of_independence" title="Assyrian war of independence">
       Assyrian war of independence
      </a>
      was fought during
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      . For a time, the Assyrians fought successfully against overwhelming numbers, scoring a number of victories over the Ottomans and Kurds, and also hostile Arab and Iranian groups; then their
      <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">
       Russian
      </a>
      allies left the war following the
      <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">
       Russian Revolution
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">
       Armenian
      </a>
      resistance broke. The Assyrians were left cut off, surrounded, and without supplies, forcing those in Asia Minor and Northwest Iran to fight their way, with civilians in tow, to the safety of
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       British
      </a>
      lines and their fellow Assyrians in northern Iraq. The sizeable Assyrian presence in south eastern Anatolia which had endured for over four millennia was thus reduced to no more than 15,000 by the end of World War I.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Levies" title="Assyrian Levies">
       Assyrian Levies
      </a>
      were founded by the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       British
      </a>
      in 1928, with ancient Assyrian military rankings, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rab-shakeh" title="Rab-shakeh">
       Rab-shakeh
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rab-talia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rab-talia (page does not exist)">
       Rab-talia
      </a>
      and Tartan, being revived for the first time in millennia for this force. The Assyrians were prized by the British rulers for their fighting qualities, loyalty, bravery and discipline, and were used to help the British put down insurrections among the Arabs and Kurds, guard the borders with Iran and Turkey and protect British military installations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After Iraq was granted independence by the British in 1933, the Assyrians suffered the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simele_Massacre" title="Simele Massacre">
       Simele Massacre
      </a>
      , where thousands of unarmed villagers (men, women and children) were slaughtered by joint Arab-Kurdish forces of the Iraqi Army. These massacres followed a clash between Assyrian tribesmen and the Iraqi army, where the Iraqi forces suffered a defeat after trying to disarm the Assyrians, whom they feared would attempt to secede from Iraq. Armed Assyrian Levies were prevented by the British from going to the aid of these civilians.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrians were allied with the British during
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      , with eleven Assyrian companies seeing action in
      <a href="/wiki/Palestine" title="Palestine">
       Palestine
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">
       Israel
      </a>
      and another four serving in
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      . The Parachute Company was attached to the Royal Marine Commando and was involved in fighting in
      <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">
       Albania
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
       Italy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">
       Greece
      </a>
      . Assyrians played a major role in the victory over Arab-Iraqi forces at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Habbaniya" title="Battle of Habbaniya">
       Battle of Habbaniya
      </a>
      in 1941, when the Iraqi government decided to join WW2 on the side of
      <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">
       Nazi Germany
      </a>
      . The British presence in Iraq lasted until 1955, and Assyrian Levies remained attached to British forces until this time.
     </p>
     <p>
      The period from the 1940s through to 1963 saw a period of respite for the Assyrians. The regime of President Kassim in particular saw the Assyrians accepted into mainstream society. Many urban Assyrians became successful businessmen, others were well represented in politics and the military, their towns and villages flourished undisturbed, and Assyrians came to excel, and be over represented in sports such as Boxing, Football, Athletics, Wrestling and Swimming.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_wedding,_Mechelen.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2751" data-file-width="2865" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg/180px-Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg/270px-Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg/360px-Assyrian_wedding%2C_Mechelen.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_wedding,_Mechelen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Assyrian wedding, diaspora in Belgium
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      However in 1963, the
      <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party" title="Ba'ath Party">
       Ba'ath Party
      </a>
      took power by force in Iraq. The Baathists, though secular, were
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Arab_Nationalists&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Arab Nationalists (page does not exist)">
       Arab Nationalists
      </a>
      , and set about attempting to
      <i>
       Arabize
      </i>
      the many non Arab peoples of Iraq, including the Assyrians. Other ethnic groups targeted for forced
      <i>
       Arabization
      </i>
      included
      <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">
       Kurds
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">
       Armenians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Turkmen" title="Iraqi Turkmen">
       Turcomans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandeans" title="Mandeans">
       Mandeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yezidi" title="Yezidi">
       Yezidi
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shabaki" title="Shabaki">
       Shabaki
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kawliya" title="Kawliya">
       Kawliya
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
       Persians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">
       Circassians
      </a>
      . This policy included refusing to acknowledge the Assyrians as an ethnic group, banning the publication of written material in Eastern Aramaic, and banning its teaching in schools, banning parents giving Assyrian names to their children, banning Assyrian political parties, taking control of Assyrian churches, attempting to divide Assyrians on denominational lines (e.g. Assyrian Church of the East vs Chaldean Catholic Church vs Syriac Orthodox) and forced relocations of Assyrians from their traditional homelands to major cities.
     </p>
     <p>
      In response to Baathist persecution, the Assyrians of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zowaa" title="Zowaa">
       Zowaa
      </a>
      movement within the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Democratic_Movement" title="Assyrian Democratic Movement">
       Assyrian Democratic Movement
      </a>
      took up armed struggle against the Iraqi regime in 1982 under the leadership of
      <a href="/wiki/Yonadam_Kanna" title="Yonadam Kanna">
       Yonadam Kanna
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and then joined up with the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Iraqi-Kurdistan_Front&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Iraqi-Kurdistan Front (page does not exist)">
       IKF
      </a>
      in early 1990s. Yonadam Kanna in particular was a target of the
      <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">
       Saddam Hussein
      </a>
      Ba'ath regime for many years.
     </p>
     <p>
      The policies of the Bathists have also long been mirrored in Turkey, whose governments have refused to acknowledge the Assyrians as an ethnic group since the 1920s, and have attempted to
      <i>
       Turkify
      </i>
      the Assyrians by calling them
      <i>
       Semitic Turks
      </i>
      and forcing them to adopt Turkic names. In Syria too, the Assyrian/Syriac Christians have faced pressure to identify as
      <i>
       Arab Christians
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Many persecutions have befallen the Assyrians since, such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anfal" title="Anfal">
       Anfal
      </a>
      campaign and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baathist" title="Baathist">
       Baathist
      </a>
      , Arab and Kurdish nationalist and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamist" title="Islamist">
       Islamist
      </a>
      persecutions.
     </p>
     <p>
      In recent years, the Assyrians in northern Iraq and north east Syria have become the target of extreme unprovoked
      <a href="/wiki/Islamic_terrorism" title="Islamic terrorism">
       Islamic terrorism
      </a>
      . As a result, Assyrians have taken up arms, alongside other groups (such as the Kurds, Turcomans and Armenians) in response to unprovoked attacks by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Qaeda" title="Al Qaeda">
       Al Qaeda
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIS" title="ISIS">
       ISIS
      </a>
      /
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISIL" title="ISIL">
       ISIL
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nusra_Front" title="Nusra Front">
       Nusra Front
      </a>
      and other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrorist" title="Terrorist">
       terrorist
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Fundamentalist" title="Islamic Fundamentalist">
       Islamic Fundamentalist
      </a>
      groups. In 2014 Islamic terrorists of ISIS attacked Assyrian towns and villages in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_Homeland" title="Assyrian Homeland">
       Assyrian Homeland
      </a>
      of northern Iraq, together with cities such as
      <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">
       Mosul
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">
       Kirkuk
      </a>
      which have large Assyrian populations. There have been reports of atrocities committed by ISIS terrorists since, including; beheadings, crucifixions, child murders, rape, forced conversions,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_Cleansing" title="Ethnic Cleansing">
       Ethnic Cleansing
      </a>
      , robbery, and extortion in the form of illegal taxes levied upon non Muslims. Assyrians in Iraq have responded by forming armed militias to defend their territories.
     </p>
     <p>
      Thus far, the only people who have been attested with a high level of genetic, historical, linguistic and cultural research to be the descendants of the ancient Mesopotamians are the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">
       Christians
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
       Iraq
      </a>
      and its surrounding areas in north west
      <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
       Iran
      </a>
      , north east
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      and south eastern
      <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
       Turkey
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_continuity" title="Assyrian continuity">
       Assyrian continuity
      </a>
      ), although others have made unsubstantiated claims of continuity. Assyria continued to exist as a geopolitical entity until the Arab-Islamic conquest in the mid-7th century, and Assyrian identity, personal, family and tribal names, and both spoken and written evolutions of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian" title="Mesopotamian">
       Mesopotamian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">
       Aramaic
      </a>
      (which still contain many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akkadian" title="Akkadian">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      loan words and an Akkadian grammatical structure) have survived among the Assyrian people from ancient times to this day. (see
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian people
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_religion">
       Assyrian religion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Assyrian religion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The Assyrians, like the rest of the Mesopotamian peoples, followed the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumero-Akkadian" title="Sumero-Akkadian">
       Sumero-Akkadian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_Religion" title="Mesopotamian Religion">
       Mesopotamian Religion
      </a>
      , with the national god
      <a href="/wiki/Ashur_(god)" title="Ashur (god)">
       Ashur
      </a>
      having pride of place at the head of the pantheon. This religion survived in Assyria from c. 3500 BC through to its gradual decline in the face of Christianity between the 1st and 10th centuries AD.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineveh.com_60-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nineveh.com-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other major gods within the pantheon were
      <a href="/wiki/Anu" title="Anu">
       Anu
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">
       Baal
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ea_(Babylonian_god)" title="Ea (Babylonian god)">
       Ea
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Enlil" title="Enlil">
       Enlil
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" title="Ishtar">
       Ishtar
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Astarte" title="Astarte">
       Astarte
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">
       Shamash
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tammuz_(deity)" title="Tammuz (deity)">
       Tammuz
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Adad" title="Adad">
       Adad
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">
       Hadad
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sin_(mythology)" title="Sin (mythology)">
       Sin (Nanna)
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dagon" title="Dagon">
       Dagan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ninurta" title="Ninurta">
       Ninurta
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nisroch" title="Nisroch">
       Nisroch
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nergal" title="Nergal">
       Nergal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tiamat" title="Tiamat">
       Tiamat
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ninlil" title="Ninlil">
       Ninlil
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mullissu" title="Mullissu">
       Mullissu
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Zababa" title="Zababa">
       Zababa
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/El_(deity)" title="El (deity)">
       El
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Native religion was still strongly followed at least until the 4th century AD, and survived in pockets until at least the 10th century AD,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineveh.com_60-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-nineveh.com-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although Assyrians had begun to adopt
      <a href="/wiki/East_Syrian_Rite" title="East Syrian Rite">
       Eastern Rite
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      (as well as for a time
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manicheanism" title="Manicheanism">
       Manicheanism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">
       Gnosticism
      </a>
      ) which, like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac" title="Syriac">
       Syriac
      </a>
      literature, had its birthplace in Assyria between the 1st and 3rd centuries AD. Assyrians today are exclusively
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christian
      </a>
      , with most following the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">
       Assyrian Church of the East
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Catholic_Church" title="Chaldean Catholic Church">
       Chaldean Catholic Church
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Church_of_the_East" title="Ancient Church of the East">
       Ancient Church of the East
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox" title="Syriac Orthodox">
       Syriac Orthodox
      </a>
      churches.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Language">
       Language
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Language">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      During the 3rd millennium BC, a very intimate cultural symbiosis developed between the
      <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">
       Sumerians
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadians
      </a>
      , which included widespread
      <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bilingualism" title="wikt:bilingualism">
       bilingualism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_7-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The influence of
      <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">
       Sumerian
      </a>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      (and vice versa) is evident in all areas, from lexical borrowing on a massive scale, to syntactic, morphological, and phonological convergence.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_7-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has prompted scholars to refer to Sumerian and Akkadian in the 3rd millennium BC as a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">
        sprachbund
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deutscher_7-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deutscher-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      gradually replaced Sumerian as the spoken language of Mesopotamia somewhere around the turn of the 3rd and the 2nd millennium BC (the exact dating being a matter of debate),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woods_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-woods-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but Sumerian continued to be used as a sacred, ceremonial, literary and scientific language in Mesopotamia until the 1st century AD.
     </p>
     <p>
      In ancient times, Assyrians spoke a dialect of the
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadian language
      </a>
      , an eastern branch of the
      <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">
       Semitic languages
      </a>
      . The first inscriptions, called Old Assyrian (OA), were made in the Old Assyrian period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the Neo-Assyrian period the
      <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">
       Aramaic language
      </a>
      became increasingly common,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      more so than
      <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">
       Akkadian
      </a>
      this was thought to be largely due to the mass deportations undertaken by Assyrian kings,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in which large Aramaic-speaking populations, conquered by the Assyrians, were relocated to Assyria and interbred with the Assyrians. The ancient Assyrians also used the
      <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">
       Sumerian language
      </a>
      in their literature and liturgy,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382_72-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although to a more limited extent in the Middle- and Neo-Assyrian periods, when Akkadian became the main literary language.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382_72-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The destruction of the Assyrian capitals of Nineveh and Assur by the Babylonians, Medes and their allies, ensured that much of the bilingual elite (but not all) were wiped out. By the 7th century BC, much of the Assyrian population used Akkadian influenced Eastern Aramaic and not Akkadian itself. The last Akkadian inscriptions in Mesopotamia date from the 1st century AD. However, Eastern Aramaic dialects, as well as Akkadian and Mesopotamian Aramaic personal and family names, still survive to this day among
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrians
      </a>
      in the regions of northern
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
       Iraq
      </a>
      , southeast
      <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
       Turkey
      </a>
      , northwest
      <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
       Iran
      </a>
      and northeast
      <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
       Syria
      </a>
      that constituted old Assyria.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382_72-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux.2C_Ancient_Iraq_p382-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After 90 years of effort, the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">
       University of Chicago
      </a>
      has published an Assyrian Dictionary, whose form is more
      <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">
       encyclopedia
      </a>
      in style than
      <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">
       dictionary
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_sciences">
       Arts and sciences
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Arts and sciences">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Mesopotamia" title="Art of Mesopotamia">
       Art of Mesopotamia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Mesopotamia" title="Architecture of Mesopotamia">
       Architecture of Mesopotamia
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg/220px-Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg/330px-Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg/440px-Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Transport_of_cedar_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Relief from Assyrian capital of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dur_Sharrukin" title="Dur Sharrukin">
         Dur Sharrukin
        </a>
        , showing transport of Lebanese cedar (8th century BC)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Assyrian art preserved to the present day predominantly dates to the Neo-Assyrian period. Art depicting battle scenes, and occasionally the impaling of whole villages in gory detail, was intended to show the power of the emperor, and was generally made for propaganda purposes. These stone reliefs lined the walls in the royal palaces where foreigners were received by the king. Other stone reliefs depict the king with different deities and conducting religious ceremonies. Many stone reliefs were discovered in the royal palaces at
      <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">
       Nimrud
      </a>
      (Kalhu) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khorsabad" title="Khorsabad">
       Khorsabad
      </a>
      (Dur-Sharrukin). A rare discovery of metal plates belonging to wooden doors was made at
      <a href="/wiki/Balawat" title="Balawat">
       Balawat
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imgur-Enlil" title="Imgur-Enlil">
       Imgur-Enlil
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="811" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg/220px-S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg/330px-S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg/440px-S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S03_06_01_017_image_2350.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Assyria. Head of winged bull, 9th c. B.C.; Brooklyn Museum Brooklyn Museum Archives, Goodyear Archival Collection
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Assyrian sculpture reached a high level of refinement in the Neo-Assyrian period. One prominent example is the winged bull
      <i>
       Lamassu
      </i>
      , or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shedu" title="Shedu">
       shedu
      </a>
      that guard the entrances to the king's court. These were apotropaic meaning they were intended to ward off evil. C. W. Ceram states in
      <i>
       The March of Archaeology
      </i>
      that
      <i>
       lamassi
      </i>
      were typically sculpted with five legs so that four legs were always visible, whether the image were viewed frontally or in profile.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although works of precious gems and metals usually do not survive the ravages of time, some fine pieces of Assyrian jewelry were found in royal tombs at Nimrud.
     </p>
     <p>
      There is ongoing discussion among academics over the nature of the
      <a href="/wiki/Nimrud_lens" title="Nimrud lens">
       Nimrud lens
      </a>
      , a piece of
      <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">
       quartz
      </a>
      unearthed by
      <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">
       Austen Henry Layard
      </a>
      in 1850, in the Nimrud palace complex in northern
      <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">
       Iraq
      </a>
      . A small minority believe that it is evidence for the existence of ancient Assyrian telescopes, which could explain the great accuracy of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_astronomy" title="Assyrian astronomy">
       Assyrian astronomy
      </a>
      . Other suggestions include its use as a magnifying glass for jewellers, or as a decorative furniture inlay. The Nimrud Lens is held in the British Museum.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mu.C5.BFeum_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mu.C5.BFeum-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrians were also innovative in military technology, with the use of heavy cavalry, sappers, siege engines etc.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
       Legacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Assyria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Legacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">
       Achaemenid Assyria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Assyriology" title="Assyriology">
       Assyriology
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrianism" title="Assyrianism">
       Assyrianism
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Layard_Nineveh.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="598" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/240px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/360px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Layard_Nineveh.jpg/480px-Layard_Nineveh.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Layard_Nineveh.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">
         Austen Henry Layard
        </a>
        in Nineveh, 1852
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">
       Achaemenid
      </a>
      Assyria (539330 BC) retained a separate identity (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athura" title="Athura">
       Athura
      </a>
      ), official correspondence being in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Aramaic" title="Imperial Aramaic">
       Imperial Aramaic
      </a>
      , and there was even a determined revolt of the two Assyrian provinces of Mada and Athura in 520 BC. Under
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid" title="Seleucid">
       Seleucid
      </a>
      rule (330 BC  approximately 150 BC), however,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">
       Aramaic
      </a>
      gave way to
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      as the official administrative language. Aramaic was marginalised as an official language, but remained spoken in both Assyria and Babylonia by the general populace. It also remained the spoken tongue of the indigenous Assyrian/Babylonian citizens of all Mesopotamia under Persian, Greek and Roman rule, and indeed well into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">
       Arab
      </a>
      period it was still the language of the majority, particularly in the north of Mesopotamia, surviving to this day among the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian
      </a>
      Christians.
     </p>
     <p>
      Between 150 BC and 226 AD, Assyria changed hands between the
      <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">
       Parthian
      </a>
      Iranians and Romans (
      <a href="/wiki/Assyria_(Roman_province)" title="Assyria (Roman province)">
       Roman Province of Assyria
      </a>
      ) until coming under the rule of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">
       Sassanid
      </a>
      Persia in 226651 AD, where it was known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asuristan" title="Asuristan">
       Asuristan
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      A number of at least partly neo-Assyrian kingdoms existed in the area between in the late classical and early Christian period also;
      <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">
       Adiabene
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">
       Hatra
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">
       Osroene
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia_in_Classical_literature" title="Mesopotamia in Classical literature">
       Classical historiographers
      </a>
      and Biblical writers had only retained a fragmented, very dim and often inaccurate picture of Assyria. It was remembered that there had been an Assyrian empire predating the Persian one, but all particulars were lost. Thus Jerome's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Chronicon_(Jerome)" title="Chronicon (Jerome)">
        Chronicon
       </a>
      </i>
      lists 36 kings of the Assyrians, beginning with
      <a href="/wiki/Ninus" title="Ninus">
       Ninus
      </a>
      , son of
      <a href="/wiki/Belus_(Assyrian)" title="Belus (Assyrian)">
       Belus
      </a>
      , down to
      <a href="/wiki/Sardanapalus" title="Sardanapalus">
       Sardanapalus
      </a>
      , the last king of the Assyrians before the empire fell to
      <a href="/wiki/Arbaces" title="Arbaces">
       Arbaces
      </a>
      the Median. Almost none of these have been substantiated as historical, with the exception of the Neo-Assyrian and Babylonian rulers listed in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_Canon" title="Ptolemy's Canon">
       Ptolemy's Canon
      </a>
      , beginning with
      <a href="/wiki/Nabonassar" title="Nabonassar">
       Nabonassar
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Mesopotamian empires such as the Akkadian Empire, Babylonian Empire, Middle Assyrian Empire, Neo Assyrian Empire and Neo Babylonian Empire asserted Mesopotamian dominance from the
      <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">
       Caucasus Mountains
      </a>
      to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">
       Arabia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      , and from
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      and the east
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">
       Mediterranean
      </a>
      to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">
       Persia
      </a>
      . Thus the influence exerted by the Babylonian-Assyrian religion was particularly profound on other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semites" title="Semites">
       Semites
      </a>
      , including the
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">
       Hebrews
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chaldea" title="Chaldea">
       Chaldeans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canaanites" title="Canaanites">
       Canaanites
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">
       Phoenicians
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
       Arabs
      </a>
      , while their astral theology affected the ancient world in general, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">
       Persians
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">
       Greeks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">
       Armenians
      </a>
      and the later
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
       Romans
      </a>
      . The impetus to the purification of the old
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic" title="Semitic">
       Semitic
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic_religions" title="Polytheistic religions">
       polytheistic religions
      </a>
      to which the
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">
       Hebrews
      </a>
      for a long time clung in common with their fellowsthe various branches of nomadic
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Arameans
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">
       Arabs
      </a>
      was largely furnished by the remarkable civilization unfolded in the Euphrates valley and in many of the traditions, myths and legends embodied in the Old Testament; traces of direct adaptation from and responses to Babylonia and Assyria may be discerned, while the indirect influences in the domain of the prophetical books, as also in the
      <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">
       Psalms
      </a>
      and in the so-called "wisdom literature", are even more noteworthy. Stories in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">
       Tanakh
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old Testament
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">
       Quran
      </a>
      such as; the
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">
       Genesis
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Creation_narrative" title="Creation narrative">
       creation narrative
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Babel" title="Tower of Babel">
       Tower of Babel
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_Flood" title="The Great Flood">
       The Great Flood
      </a>
      and the book of
      <a href="/wiki/Esther" title="Esther">
       Esther
      </a>
      , as well as various biblical characters such as
      <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">
       Noah
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nimrod" title="Nimrod">
       Nimrod
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lilith" title="Lilith">
       Lilith
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asnapper" title="Asnapper">
       Asnapper
      </a>
      bear clear influence from Assyria and Babylonia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Even when we reach the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      period, we have not passed entirely beyond the sphere of Babylonian-Assyrian influences. In such a movement as early
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">
       gnosticism
      </a>
      , Assyrio-Babylonian elementsmodified, to be sure, and transformedare largely present, while the growth of an apocalyptic literature is ascribed with apparent justice by many scholars to the recrudescence of views, the ultimate source of which is to be found in the astral-theology of the Babylonian and Assyrian Priests.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Assyrians began to form and adopt a distinct
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">
       Eastern Rite
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      , with its accompanying
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_literature" title="Syriac literature">
       Syriac literature
      </a>
      , between the 1st and 3rd centuries AD, however native religion was still alive and well into the 4th century AD, and pockets survived into the 10th century AD and possibly as late as the 17th century in Mardin. However, the religion is now dead, and the indigenous Assyrian (a.k.a. Chaldo-Assyrian) people, though still retaining Eastern Aramaic dialects as a mother tongue, are now wholly Christian.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_discovery_of_Babylonia_and_Assyria" title="Modern discovery of Babylonia and Assyria">
       modern discovery of Babylonia and Assyria
      </a>
      begins with excavations in
      <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">
       Nineveh
      </a>
      in 1845, which revealed the
      <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Ashurbanipal" title="Library of Ashurbanipal">
       Library of Ashurbanipal
      </a>
      . Decipherment of
      <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">
       cuneiform
      </a>
      was a formidable task that took more than a decade; but, by 1857, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Asiatic_Society" title="Royal Asiatic Society">
       Royal Asiatic Society
      </a>
      was convinced that reliable reading of cuneiform texts was possible.
      <a href="/wiki/Assyriology" title="Assyriology">
       Assyriology
      </a>
      has since pieced together the formerly largely forgotten history of Mesopotamia. In the wake of the archaeological and philological
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rediscovery_of_ancient_Assyria" title="Rediscovery of ancient Assyria">
       rediscovery of ancient Assyria
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_nationalism" title="Assyrian nationalism">
       Assyrian nationalism
      </a>
      became increasingly popular among the surviving remnants of the
      <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">
       Assyrian people
      </a>
      , and has come to strongly identify with ancient Assyria.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>

      </span>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
       References
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [

       <li id="cite_note-H.W.F._Saggs-5">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-H.W.F._Saggs_5-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Saggs notes that: "the destruction of the Assyrian empire did not wipe out its population. They were predominantly peasant farmers and, since Assyria contains some of the best wheat land in the Near East, their descendants would, as opportunity permitted, build new villages over the old cities and carry on with agricultural life, remembering traditions of the former cities. After seven or eight centuries and various vicissitudes, these people became Christians" (
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Might_That_Was_Assyria" title="The Might That Was Assyria">
           The Might That Was Assyria
          </a>
         </i>
         , p. 290).
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-AK2013-33">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-AK2013_33-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation" id="CITEREFKillebrew2013">
          Killebrew, Ann E. (2013),
          <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=gBCl2IQfNioC&amp;pg=PA1#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">
           "The Philistines and Other "Sea Peoples" in Text and Archaeology"
          </a>
          ,
          <i>
           Society of Biblical Literature Archaeology and biblical studies
          </i>
          (Society of Biblical Lit)
          <b>
           15
          </b>
          : 2,
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781589837218" title="Special:BookSources/9781589837218">
           9781589837218
          </a>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyria&amp;rft.atitle=The+Philistines+and+Other+%22Sea+Peoples%22+in+Text+and+Archaeology&amp;rft.aufirst=Ann+E.&amp;rft.au=Killebrew%2C+Ann+E.&amp;rft.aulast=Killebrew&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DgBCl2IQfNioC%26pg%3DPA1%23v%3Donepage%26q%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=9781589837218&amp;rft.jtitle=Society+of+Biblical+Literature+Archaeology+and+biblical+studies&amp;rft.pages=2&amp;rft.pub=Society+of+Biblical+Lit&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=15">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         . Quote: "First coined in 1881 by the French Egyptologist G. Maspero (1896), the somewhat misleading term "Sea Peoples" encompasses the ethnonyms Lukka, Sherden, Shekelesh, Teresh, Eqwesh, Denyen, Sikil / Tjekker, Weshesh, and Peleset (Philistines). [Footnote: The modern term "Sea Peoples" refers to peoples that appear in several New Kingdom Egyptian texts as originating from "islands" (tables 1-2; Adams and Cohen, this volume; see, e.g.,
         <a href="/wiki/Robert_Drews" title="Robert Drews">
          Drews
         </a>
         1993, 57 for a summary). The use of quotation marks in association with the term "Sea Peoples" in our title is intended to draw attention to the problematic nature of this commonly used term. It is noteworthy that the designation "of the sea" appears only in relation to the Sherden, Shekelesh, and Eqwesh. Subsequently, this term was applied somewhat indiscriminately to several additional ethnonyms, including the Philistines, who are portrayed in their earliest appearance as invaders from the north during the reigns of Merenptah and Ramesses Ill (see, e.g., Sandars 1978; Redford 1992, 243, n. 14; for a recent review of the primary and secondary literature, see Woudhuizen 2006). Hencefore the term Sea Peoples will appear without quotation marks.]"
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-Drews48-34">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-Drews48_34-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=bFpK6aXEWN8C&amp;pg=PA48&amp;lpg=PA48" rel="nofollow">
          The End of the Bronze Age: Changes in Warfare and the Catastrophe Ca. 1200 B.C., Robert Drews, p4861
         </a>
         Quote: "The thesis that a great "migration of the Sea Peoples" occurred ca. 1200 B.C. is supposedly based on Egyptian inscriptions, one from the reign of Merneptah and another from the reign of Ramesses III. Yet in the inscriptions themselves such a migration nowhere appears. After reviewing what the Egyptian texts have to say about 'the sea peoples', one Egyptologist (Wolfgang Helck) recently remarked that although some things are unclear, "eins ist aber sicher: Nach den agyptischen Texten haben wir es nicht mit einer 'Volkerwanderung' zu tun." Thus the migration hypothesis is based not on the inscriptions themselves but on their interpretation."
        </span>
       </li>
